Preface 
 
This master’s thesis was prepared from 3 February 2025 to 24 July 2025 at the Department DTU 
Engineering Technology, at the Technical University of Denmark in partial fulfilment of the Master 
of Science degree. The project is accredited with 30 ECTS. 
 
I hereby declare that the thesis submitted is my own work. All direct or indirect sources used are

==PAGE:4==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
Abstract 
 
To what extent are green materials environmentally superior to fossil-based alternatives in food 
packaging? The study answers this question by focusing on one specific single-use plastic food 
packaging, namely very lightweight plastic carrier bags (VLPCBs). The VLPCBs are commonly 
used for fresh produce and are characterized by a wall thickness of 15µm according to the Euro-
pean Union (EU) Directive 2015/720. In 2022 alone, Denmark consumed approximately 
78,566,187 of these VLPCBs. At that scale, VLPCBs are contributing significantly to plastic waste 
and researchers and practitioners alike propose green materials as more sustainable alternatives. 
Drawing on these insights, this master’s thesis conducts a comparative life cycle assessment in 
accordance with ISO14040. More specifically, the study compares fossil-based low-density poly-
ethylene (LDPE) with the green materials polylactic acid and thermoplastic starch by linking the 
environmental impacts of these materials to the Planetary Boundaries (PB) framework. This is 
done by using the PB Life Cycle Impact Assessment Method proposed by Ryberg et al. (2018). 
Therefore, the study corrects the PB-LCIA2.03_OpenLCA dataset and updates it to align it with 
the current version 3.11 (APOS) of ecoinvent and openLCA v2.4.1. Based on the PB-LCIA, an 
absolute environmental sustainability assessment (AESA) is conducted. The results reveal that 
none of the assessed materials can be considered absolutely sustainable. LDPE outperforms the 
green materials. It meets sustainability criteria in three impact categories, compared to just two 
for the green alternatives. These findings are robust to several sensitivity tests (alternative allo-
cation method, one-time approach, and scenario analyses) and uncertainty analysis using Monte 
Carlo simulations. 
 
Keywords: Very lightweight plastic carrier bags, green materials, absolute environmental sus-
tainability assessment

Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
1 
1. Introduction 
In 2024, the global food packaging market was valued at 400.29 billion USD and is projected to 
grow to 562.38 billion USD by 2030, representing an increase of 40.48 %. Plastic holds the largest 
market share of all food packaging materials with 30.90 % in 2024 (Grand View Research, 2025). 
While plastics offer economic, technical, and functional advantages over other materials (Avery 
et al., 2025; Bachmann, 2024; Cruz et al., 2022; Mendes & Pedersen, 2021; Ncube et al., 2020), 
fossil-based plastics in particular are associated with substantial environmental drawbacks. It re-
mains one of the most pressing environmental challenges today, contributing to climate change 
through its production and persisting in the environment for a very long time (OECD, 2025). 
 
To address the environmental impact of plastic food packaging, different strategies are pursued. 
These include bans of single-use plastic, circular economy concepts, advancements in recycling 
innovations, and reusable packaging concepts (Thapliyal et al., 2024). Another recent approach 
is the use of green materials in food packaging applications (Amin et al., 2021; Coelho & de Souza 
Henriques, 2025; H. Kumar et al., 2022; Maurizzi et al., 2022). The wide-reaching assumption is 
that these green materials are by their very nature more sustainable than conventional fossil-
based materials (Cruz et al., 2022; Mendes & Pedersen, 2021; Pradhan, 2021). However, this 
assumption often overlooks that green materials also have environmental burdens across their 
life cycle (Avery et al., 2025; Islam et al., 2024; Mendes & Pedersen, 2021). It is thus still unre-
solved whether they offer an environmentally superior alternative to conventional fossil-based 
plastics (Coelho & de Souza Henriques, 2025).  
 
This study aims to contribute to this debate by focusing on a specific type of plastic food packag-
ing, very lightweight plastic carrier bags (VLPCBs). It is investigated whether polylactic acid (PLA) 
and thermoplastic starch (TPS1 and TPS2), as green materials, offer more eco-friendly alterna-
tives to conventional fossil-based low-density polyethylene (LDPE). VLPCBs are among the most 
widely used single-use food packaging items globally (Nielsen et al., 2019; United Nations Envi-
ronment Program, 2020), and are encountered daily in grocery stores. Because of their single-
use nature and significant contribution to plastic waste, Denmark introduced an Extended Pro-
ducer Responsibility (EPR) Scheme, effective 1 January 2025. Through this scheme, manufac-
turers must finance the collection and treatment of waste generated by these products, which 
pushes them to use more environmentally friendly materials (Danish Environmental Protection 
Agency, 2025b). Hence, identifying truly sustainable material alternatives for VLPCBs is not only 
environmentally necessary but also economically relevant.  
 
To achieve this, the study concentrates on the Danish market to carry out an Absolute Environ-
mental Sustainability Assessment (AESA). Using information from an in-depth analysis of the 
Danish VLPCB market, a comprehensive Life Cycle Inventory (LCI) was modelled using data from 
ecoinvent v3.11 (Allocation at Point of Substitution (APOS)) (Wernet et al., 2016) in openLCA 
v2.4.1 (GreenDelta GmbH, 2025b). Subsequently, the Life Cycle Impact Assessment (LCIA) was 
carried out following the Planetary Boundaries LCIA (PB-LCIA) methodology developed by 
Ryberg, Owsianiak, Richardson, et al. (2018). For this step, the PB-LCIA dataset developed by 
Nørrekær Lund, Schjerbeck, and Winther Schor (2023) was extensively adapted to fit the current 
versions of ecoinvent and openLCA. Through PB-LCIA, the life cycle impacts of each material 
can be linked to the assigned share of safe operating space (SoSOS), enabling the AESA. 
==PAGE:12==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
2 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
The analysis reveals that no materials (LDPE, PLA, TPS1, or TPS2) can be deemed absolutely 
sustainable. LDPE performs best in 9 out of 12 environmental impact categories, exhibiting the 
lowest overall impacts and exceeding its allocated share of the safe operating space the least. It 
meets sustainability criteria in 3 categories, compared to just 2 for the green alternatives. To test 
these findings’ robustness, sensitivity and uncertainty analyses were conducted. The sensitivity 
analysis included three elements: (1) an alternative allocation method based on recent AESA 
literature and European guidelines, (2) a one-time approach to identifying key parameters, and 
(3) a scenario analysis. Although these tests yielded some nuanced insights, the overall outcome 
remained consistent. LDPE outperforms the bio-based options. This conclusion was further rein-
forced by uncertainty analysis using Monte Carlo simulations. 
 
These findings contribute to the literature in at least three significant ways. First, to the best of my 
knowledge, no peer-reviewed study has conducted an AESA specifically for food packaging. This 
research addresses that gap by focusing on VLPCBs. Second, the study is tailored to the Danish 
context. Since results from other countries cannot be generalized to Denmark, it is crucial to adapt 
the analysis to national conditions to assess whether green materials perform better or worse 
than conventional plastics from a sustainability perspective. Third, the study makes a methodo-
logical contribution. The PB-LCIA dataset, created by Ryberg, Owsianiak, Richardson, et al. 
(2018) for SimaPro and later converted for openLCA by Nørrekær Lund, Schjerbeck, and Winther 
Schor (2023), was limited. Specifically, some of the characterization factors (CFs) used had been 
adjusted for Ryberg et al.'s (2020) specific case study and transferred without revision. This led 
to inconsistencies, such as system structures and flows that were incompatible with the ecoinvent 
database. As part of this study, these flows were corrected so that the updated dataset can now 
be used in openLCA without any restrictions, enabling future AESA studies beyond the scope of 
VLPCBs in the Danish context. 
 
The thesis is structured into several sections. Section 2 outlines the broader objective of the study, 
followed by section 3, which presents the state of the art. In the first step, food packaging and 
VLPCBs are introduced (3.1) before discussing the environmental impacts of VLPCBs and their 
regulatory consequences (3.2). Section 3.3 introduces alternative packaging materials, namely 
PLA and Starch, and section 3.4 summarizes existing LCAs on VLPCBs for the green materials. 
This is followed by an explanation of the Planetary Boundary framework (3.5) and the AESA 
framework (3.6). Following the comprehensive literature review, the study examines the packag-
ing materials and methods applied (4 and 4.1). The goal and scope are outlined (4.2), followed 
by the LCI (4.3), PB-LCIA (4.4), and the allocation of the SoSOS (4.6). Section five presents the 
results (5.1), followed by sensitivity (5.2) and uncertainty analyses (5.3), and interprets the find-
ings in light of existing studies (5.4). Finally, section six concludes with a summary of the thesis, 
its implications, and possible directions for future research. 
 
 
==PAGE:13==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
3 
2. Objective 
This master's thesis aims to assess whether green materials used for VLPCBs can offer an envi-
ronmentally superior alternative to fossil-based materials. To answer this question, a comparative 
LCA is conducted, focusing on three green materials, PLA, TPS1, and TPS2, and compares them 
against the status quo, LDPE. 
In contrast to most comparative LCAs, this study applies the AESA framework. This approach 
evaluates not only the relative performance of materials but also whether their environmental 
impacts remain within the ecological limits defined by the PB framework, by linking the life cycle 
impacts of each material to their assigned SoSOS for the Danish VLPCB market. With this ap-
proach, the study aims to provide robust, science-based insights for sustainable material choices 
in the Danish food packaging context. 
In order to achieve the main objective, the following specific objectives were defined to investigate 
the absolute sustainability performance of PLA, TPS1, TPS2, and LDPE for use in VLPCBs: 
• 
Thorough analysis of the Danish VLPCB market. 
• 
Development of a comprehensive LCI for the selected materials options, based on 
the findings from the market analysis. 
• 
Exploration of the applicability of the PB-LCIA methodology for assessing food 
packaging materials at the national level. 
• 
Using two different allocation approaches to calculate the SoSOS applicable to 
VLPCBs in the Danish context. 
• 
Analysis of the absolute environmental sustainability of each material by evaluating 
whether their life cycle impacts remain within the assigned SoSOS. 
• 
Contribute to the methodological development of AESA by improving and adapting 
the PB-LCIA dataset to ensure compatibility with the current versions of ecoinvent 
and openLCA. 
By addressing these objectives, the thesis seeks to advance the scientific understanding of green 
material sustainability and the methodological toolbox for AESA in the context of food packaging. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:14==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
4 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
3. Literature review 
3.1 Food Packaging and Very Lightweight Plastic Carrier Bags 
 
Food packaging is an essential part of today’s food system. Consequently, almost every food 
product today is packaged in some form from the producer to the end consumer. This is due to 
the critical functions that packaging performs. Among the most important functions is the protec-
tion of food products1, which enables maintaining high food quality (Cruz et al., 2022; Kalpana et 
al., 2019). This is achieved by preserving the inherent attributes of the food product by protecting 
it from external factors such as humidity, light, microbial contamination, or temperature fluctua-
tions among others (Gaspar & Braga, 2023; González-López et al., 2023; Khalid & Arif, 2022). 
This protective function is necessary across the entire food supply chain to ensure that food re-
mains fresh and safe during distribution, storage, retailing, and ultimately consumption (Amin et 
al., 2021; Kalpana et al., 2019). Thereby, packaging contributes to the prevention of food loss and 
waste (Gaspar & Braga, 2023; Khalid & Arif, 2022), which represents not only an economic chal-
lenge but also a major environmental threat (cf. Aschemann-Witzel, 2016; Skaf et al., 2021). To 
meet the functional demands of food packaging, a variety of materials are employed, such as 
glass, metals, paper, plastics, or combinations thereof (González-López et al., 2023; Sundqvist-
Andberg & Åkerman, 2021).  
 
Today, plastic packaging is predominant in the food packaging industry (H. Kumar et al., 2022; 
Mendes & Pedersen, 2021; Ncube et al., 2020). The prevalent use of plastics in food packaging 
can be attributed to a combination of economic, technical and functional properties. Firstly, plastic 
is cheap, easy to process and highly versatile in manufacturing. Moreover, plastic offers favorable 
physical properties such as lightweight, transparency, temperature stability, and sufficient me-
chanical resistance. Most importantly, plastics exhibit excellent barrier properties for protecting 
food against the aforementioned external factors (Avery et al., 2025; Bachmann, 2024; Cruz et 
al., 2022; Mendes & Pedersen, 2021; Ncube et al., 2020).  
 
The most frequently used plastic materials in food packaging include low-density and high-density 
polyethylene (LDPE & HDPE), polyethylene terephthalate (PET), polypropylene (PP), polystyrene 
(PS), and polyvinyl chloride (PVC). Among these, polyethylene represents the prevailing material 
(OECD, 2022a). As the global population continuous to increase, the demand for food and re-
spective demand for packaging increases (Swetha et al., 2023). For instance, compared to 2019, 
the demand for LDPE is expected to triple by 2060 (OECD, 2022b). A substantial share of this 
growth is driven by the demand for single-use food plastic packaging (Ncube et al., 2020). A 
prominent example of single-use food plastic packaging represent lightweight plastic carrier bags 
(LPCB)2 as they are one of most used products globally (Nielsen et al., 2019; United Nations 
 
1 Apart from food protection, packaging also fulfills other functions such as commercial aspects (e.g. cost optimization) 
(Kalpana et al., 2019), communication and sales (e.g., product information and marketing), convenience (easy-to-use 
systems) (Cruz et al., 2022; Khalid & Arif, 2022), or logistics optimization (e.g., optimized packaging systems for efficient 
handling and shipping) (Thapliyal et al., 2024).  
2 The terms „lightweight plastic carrier bag“ and „single-use plastic bag“ are used interchangeably in literature (United 
Nations Environment Program, 2020). However, in this study, the terms lightweight plastic carrier bag (LPCB) respectively 
very lightweight plastic carrier bag (VLPCB) are used, as this is the official term of the EU and Danish regulations (Danish 
Environmental Protection Agency, 2025b, 2025a; Directive - 2015/720 - EN - EUR-Lex, 2015) 
==PAGE:15==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
5 
Environment Program, 2020). As the term “single-use” suggests, LPCBs are designed to be uti-
lized only once before disposal (Bisinella et al., 2018; United Nations Environment Program, 
2020). Their main purpose is to assist facilitating the transport of goods from retail to the con-
sumer’s destination (Civancik-Uslu et al., 2019). In the context of food packaging, LPCBs are 
commonly used for grocery shopping (Nielsen et al., 2019).  
 
However, distinctions persist within the category of LPCBs. According to the EU directive 
2015/720, all plastic bags are classified as LPCBs if their wall thickness is below 50 microns. In 
contrast, plastic bags with a thickness below 15 microns are classified as VLPCBs (Directive - 
2015/720 - EN - EUR-Lex, 2015). While LPCBs can also be used for other items (Alfei et al., 
2021; Bisinella et al., 2018; COWI A/S et al., 2019), VLPCBs are supplied by retailers primarily 
for packaging of fresh fruits and vegetables (Alfei et al., 2021; Directive - 2015/720 - EN - EUR-
Lex, 2015) and are typically made from LDPE (Alfei et al., 2021; Bisinella et al., 2018; COWI A/S 
et al., 2019).3 
 
3.2 Environmental Impact of VLPCBs and Regulatory Consequences 
 
While plastic represents the preferred material for food packaging applications (see Section 3.1), 
the global plastic pollution is amongst the most critical environmental challenges today. Without 
any intervention, the production of plastic and related pollution will increase by 70% by 2040 
(OECD, 2025), while the biggest portion of the global plastic pollution originates from food pack-
aging (Gaspar & Braga, 2023; González-López et al., 2023; Ncube et al., 2020). 
 
The alarming rate of plastic entering the environment is fueled by single-use plastic food packag-
ing (Boey et al., 2021; H. Kumar et al., 2022; Ncube et al., 2020). After fulfilling its function, the 
majority of single-use plastic food packaging is immediately disposed of (Versino et al., 2023). 
However, due to improper waste handling (Avery et al., 2025; Cruz et al., 2022; Mendes & Peder-
sen, 2021), a significant amount of single-use plastic food packaging ends up in the environment. 
The related environmental issues are diverse and include for instance damage to human health 
and wildlife, degradation of nature and contributes to climate change among others (Boey et al., 
2021; H. Kumar et al., 2022; Ncube et al., 2020; United Nations Environment Assembly, 2022; 
Versino et al., 2023).  
 
Also, (V)LPCBs are part of this problem, as they are a significant source of marine plastic pollution 
(Nielsen et al., 2019). Particularly, LDPE-based (V)LPCBs are problematic, as they account for 
the biggest portion of marine litter (Steensgaard et al., 2017), as this type of (V)LPCBs is the most 
used (Singh & Cooper, 2017). Indeed, (V)LPCBs are among the most frequently found plastic 
items along European beaches (Civancik-Uslu et al., 2019; European Commission, 2018).  
 
Additionally, the pollution problem is exacerbated by the properties of the (V)LPCBs. Their light-
weight and flexibility are beneficial properties for food packaging applications. Yet, these charac-
teristics make them highly dispersible in the natural environment (Alfei et al., 2021). Therefore, 
 
3 Upon request, it was confirmed that VLPCBs are typically made from LPDE. This information was confirmed by a re-
presentative of the industry association „PLAST INDUSTRIEN – Branheforening for danske plastvirksomheder“, as well 
as by a representative of the Danish Ministry of Environment and Food (Miljø- og Fødevareministeriet). Unfortunately,

==PAGE:16==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
6 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
once (V)LPCBs enter the environment, their collection becomes difficult. Due to the non-biode-
gradable properties of petroleum-based plastic (Bala et al., 2022; Boey et al., 2021), these LPDE-
based (V)LPCBs persist long-term in the environment and become a threat to flora and fauna 
(Civancik-Uslu et al., 2019). For instance, in the context of marine environment, animals may 
ingest plastic fragments or become entangled, leading to injury or death. Additionally, the accu-
mulation of plastic waste can disrupt plant life and destroy marine ecosystems (Alfei et al., 2021). 
 
To address these issues, the European Parliament and the Council of the European Union intro-
duced an EU directive ((Directive (EU) 2015/720 of the European Parliament and of the Council 
of 29 April 2015 Amending Directive 94/62/EC as Regards Reducing the Consumption of Light-
weight Plastic Carrier Bags (Text with EEA Relevance), 2015)) to reduce the consumption of 
(V)LPCBs in order to combat littering and reduce waste. This may be a necessary step since 
studies indicate that approximately 95% of food packaging is discarded immediately after use 
(World Economic Forum, 2016), with more than two-thirds not entering respective waste collec-
tion systems. In another step, the European Commission approved a directive to restrict respec-
tively ban single-use plastic (European Commission, 2018).  
 
Denmark adopted this directive into national legislation. It limits trade and use of certain plastic 
products such as cutlery, plates, and expanded polystyrene food containers, among others (Dan-
ish Environmental Protection Agency, 2025a). However, certain single-use plastic items, including 
(V)LPCBs, remain in the Danish market and continue to contribute to plastic waste in natural 
environments. To address this ongoing issue, Denmark introduced the Extended Producer Re-
sponsibility scheme for selected single-use plastic products, effective from January 1st, 2025. Un-
der this scheme the manufacturers are required to finance the collection and treatment of waste 
generated by these products found in nature and municipalities. Currently, in 2025, the EPR fee 
for (V)LCPBs is set to 2.33 DKK per kilogram and annual adjustments are expected in the up-
coming years (Danish Environmental Protection Agency, 2025b). This regulatory creates not only 
an environmental imperative but also an economic incentive for manufacturers to switch to more 
sustainable solutions. 
 
3.3 Alternative Packaging by Green Materials 
 
To address the environmental impact of food packaging, different strategies are pursued. Besides 
bans of single-use plastic, also circular economy concepts, advancements in recycling innova-
tions, and reusable packaging concepts are implemented (Thapliyal et al., 2024). Another recent 
approach is the use of green materials in food packaging applications (Amin et al., 2021; H. Kumar 
et al., 2022; Maurizzi et al., 2022). 
 
Although there is no clear consensus in academia on the exact definition of green materials (Coe-
lho & de Souza Henriques, 2025), respectively green packaging, the term is often used inter-
changeably with eco-friendly, or sustainable packaging (Wandosell et al., 2021). According to 
Kumar et al. (2022) “green materials in food packaging refer to sustainable materials that are 
being used for food packaging applications; it includes recyclable and biodegradable materials as 
well as environmental-friendly production processes to create product packaging that has a min-
imal negative environmental effect.”  
 
==PAGE:17==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
7 
Given the environmental concerns associated with conventional plastic (see Section 3.2) and the 
properties that green materials offer, they are increasingly regarded as promising candidates as 
conventional plastic substitutes by academia, industry and regulatory bodies (Bala et al., 2022; 
Coelho & de Souza Henriques, 2025; Maurizzi et al., 2022; Nanda et al., 2022; Ncube et al., 
2020). As a direct consequence, the demand for plastics made of green materials is substantially 
increasing (Statista, 2025; Yanat et al., 2025). 
 
The terms bioplastics, bio-based plastics, or biodegradable plastics are commonly used to de-
scribe alternatives to petroleum-based materials. Yet, it is important to highlight that these terms 
are used ambiguously in both the industry and literature (Aubin et al., 2022; Cazaudehore et al., 
2022; European Commission, 2025; Lackner et al., 2023; Rosenboom et al., 2022). It is thus 
essential to understand the terminology.  
 
The term bioplastic is an umbrella term that encompasses the different classes of plastics that 
possess the characteristics of being bio-based or biodegradable or both (see Figure 3-1) (Euro-
pean Commission, 2025; Lackner et al., 2023; Lambert & Wagner, 2017). The term bio-based 
refers to the raw material and feedstock used for the production of the respective plastic (Aubin 
et al., 2022; Cazaudehore et al., 2022). The raw material for bio-based plastic is of renewable 
origin and refers to biomass that is either obtained from plants, such as corn or sugarcane or from 
organic waste such as animal by-products or agricultural waste (S. S. Ali et al., 2023; Aubin et al., 
2022; Directorate-General for Environment - European Commission, 2022; Ortega et al., 2022). 
However, two important things must be noted: (1) plastics can be labeled as bio-based even if 
their production only partially includes renewable feedstock. Blends with petroleum-based feed-
stock are also permissible. (2) The term bio-based does not imply that the material is biodegrada-
ble (Aubin et al., 2022; Cazaudehore et al., 2022; Directorate-General for Environment - Euro-
pean Commission, 2022). While some bio-based plastics are biodegradable, others are not. This 
is because the chemical structure of plastic types influences their biodegradability and not the 
properties of the raw material (Goel et al., 2021). Thus, biomass-derived materials may lose their 
biodegradability while producing certain plastic types (Rosenboom et al., 2022). 
 
Biodegradability thereby refers to microorganisms’ ability to completely degrade material into its 
elementary components of water, carbon dioxide, methane, and biomass. The process of biodeg-
radation can occur in different environments (Aubin et al., 2022; Cazaudehore et al., 2022; Ros-
enboom et al., 2022). Therefore biodegradable plastics can also be divided into different classes 
(Cruz et al., 2022). These are plastics biodegradable in (a) marine environment, (b) soil under 
natural non-optimized conditions, and (c) industrial settings. 
 
Among green materials investigated for food packaging, starch as well as PLA stand out as prom-
ising alternatives to plastic packaging (Cruz et al., 2022; García-Guzmán et al., 2022; Nian et al., 
2024; Swetha et al., 2023). Due to its attractive characteristics, such as biodegradability, low cost, 
abundance, non-toxicity and barrier properties (Chen et al., 2020; Perera et al., 2023), starch has 
already found widespread application in food packaging (Khalid et al., 2021; Khalid & Arif, 2022). 
Similarly to starch, PLA is also valued for good food contact properties, its biodegradability and 
reduced energy consumption during manufacturing compared to conventional plastics (Cruz et 
al., 2022; Nazrin et al., 2020). Furthermore, PLA can be produced with existing production facili-
ties and machinery used for conventional plastics (Bala et al., 2022). In addition, the U.S. Food 
and Drug Administration categorizes PLA as “Generally Recognized as Safe”, which enhances 
==PAGE:18==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
8 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
PLA’s appeal as a green material (Boey et al., 2021; García-Guzmán et al., 2022). In 2022, plastic 
made from PLA and starch already accounted for ca. 50% of the demand globally (Statista, 2022), 
showcasing their scale-up in industrial manufacturing (Jariyasakoolroj et al., 2020).  
 
 
Figure 3-1 - Overview Bioplastics  
Source: own illustration - adapted from European Bioplastics e.V. (2022) and Rosenboom et al. (2022) 
 
Since starch and PLA are suitable for packaging fresh fruits and vegetables (Ramezani Dana & 
Ebrahimi, 2023; Sid et al., 2021; Zhao et al., 2020), and bio-plastic films can be made from both 
materials (Muñoz-Gimena et al., 2023; Perera et al., 2023; Zhang et al., 2023), they represent the 
ideal materials for this study. These films can be applied in the production of VLPCBs for fresh 
food (Markevičiūtė & Varžinskas, 2022). Furthermore, the technical and economic feasibility of 
such products has already been demonstrated by manufacturers offering VLPCBs made from 
starch or PLA (e.g. Aploksnes Latvia, Valley Northern Limited). 
 
More recently, the assumption that green materials are more sustainable and ecologically supe-
rior to conventional fossil-based materials has emerged (Atiwesh et al., 2021; Pradhan, 2021). 
For example, in the discourse around biodegradable materials, several polarized views have de-
veloped, which range from the notion that all biodegradable materials are good and all petro-
chemical materials are bad (Cruz et al., 2022) to more nuanced considerations. These consider-
ations highlight their role as potential solutions to the environmental impacts of plastic packaging 
(Mendes & Pedersen, 2021). Due to their characteristics, green materials can replace conven-
tional food packaging and thus reduce the environmental impact of the food packaging industry 
(Coelho & de Souza Henriques, 2025; Lone et al., 2021; Pradhan, 2021). 
 
==PAGE:19==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
9 
However, the terms biobased and biodegradable are insufficient to attribute sustainability or a 
lower environmental impact to green materials. In reality, green materials can impact the environ-
ment at multiple stages throughout their lifecycle. Impacts arise from raw material sourcing, man-
ufacturing, distribution, usage and end-of-life (EoL) disposal (Avery et al., 2025; Islam et al., 2024; 
Mendes & Pedersen, 2021). For instance, notable challenges emerge at the beginning of their 
life cycles. When plants (e.g., corn or sugar cane) serve as feedstock of green materials, their 
cultivation often requires significant land-use changes. Repurposing of agricultural land or defor-
estation may be necessary, which can impact biodiversity. Furthermore, the increasing cultivation 
of feedstock plants leads to more use of fertilizer and pesticides. This, in turn, leads to acidification 
and eutrophication. Other adverse effects include increased water use and soil erosion (Atiwesh 
et al., 2021; Cruz et al., 2022; Islam et al., 2024; Vanderreydt et al., 2021). 
 
In contrast, also the EoL of green materials impacts the environment. As previously noted, not all 
biodegradable materials can decompose under natural conditions. However, for those that are 
biodegradable in the environment, nature does not always provide ideal conditions for complete 
degradation. Due to sub-optimal conditions, the risk of micro-plastic pollution increases, show-
casing harmful effects similar to those of petroleum-based plastics. Furthermore, for certain prod-
ucts industrial settings are necessary. To create optimal conditions additional resources such as 
water and energy are necessary to elevate humidity and temperature levels. Moreover, large 
scale decomposition of green materials releases methane and other gases, which contribute to 
global warming. Similar effects are observed when biodegradable materials are discarded in land-
fills. Lastly, even if bio-based, the incineration of green materials can lead to releases of toxins 
into the environment, depending on the material composition (S. S. Ali et al., 2023; Atiwesh et al., 
2021; Islam et al., 2024; Kang et al., 2022).  
 
These examples underline the importance of assessing the environmental impact of green mate-
rials comprehensively across their entire life cycle (Atiwesh et al., 2021; Bishop et al., 2021; Islam 
et al., 2024), whereby particular attention should be given to the production and EoL phase (Islam 
et al., 2024). For this purpose, the use of LCA is required. It provides a comprehensive and pre-
cise evaluation of the ecological impacts throughout the entire life cycle of a product (Pradhan, 
2021).  
 
3.4 Life Cycle Assessment of PLA & Starch for VLPCBs 
 
LCA is a standardized methodology defined by International Organization for Standardization 
(ISO) for the evaluation of environmental impacts of products of processes throughout their life 
cycle (S. S. Ali et al., 2023; Islam et al., 2024; ISO, 2006). In accordance with the ISO14040 & 
ISO14044 standards, the LCA is divided into four steps (1) Goals and Scope Definition, (2) Life 
Cycle Inventory Analysis (LCI), (3) Life Cycle Impact Assessment (LCIA) und (4) Interpretation of 
LCI and LCIA. During (1) Goal and Scope definition the reason for the study as well as the studied 
system and its boundaries are described. The second step (2) LCI, involves the collection of input 
and output data of the studied system necessary to reach the previously defined study goal. In 
the third step (3) LCIA, the LCI is analyzed to quantify the environmental impacts of the studied 
==PAGE:20==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
10 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
system. Lastly in the final step (4) the interpretation of LCI and LCIA deduces results for the 
study.4 All steps are interlinked and iteratively executed throughout the LCA study.5  
 
To conduct an LCA, different software applications are available such as openLCA, SimaPro or 
Gabi. These applications help to manage and edit the extensive databases needed for the LCI 
modeling. Furthermore, within the software, models can be built and the life cycle stages visual-
ized. Lastly, the software executes the analysis and presents the results (S. S. Ali et al., 2023). 
For the LCI modeling, different data bases can be used. These data bases can hold LCI data 
and/or LCIA methods. Available databases are for example, ecoinvent, Environmental Footprints 
or exiobase (Green Delta GmbH, 2025). 
 
Relating to food packaging, LCA is frequently used to assess different materials or systems (Bala 
et al., 2022; Bremenkamp & Gallagher, 2024). Thereby, LCAs regarding food packaging can be 
divided into four subcategories: (1) studies on food packaging systems, (2) studies comparing 
alternative packaging materials, (3) studies comparing alternative packaging systems, and (4) 
studies on alternative innovative (novel) packaging systems (Bremenkamp & Gallagher, 2024). 
 
LCAs specifically addressing VLPCBs are scarce in the literature. Most existing studies focus on 
LPCBs or reusable shopping bags with greater wall-thickness designed for general retail purpose 
and not exclusively for food packaging. The earliest study in this context dates back to 2002 (De 
Bruijn et al., 2002; Gómez & Escobar, 2022). LCA studies are often embedded in a specific geo-
graphical context and assess single-use LDPE and HDPE bags as the status quo. In recent years, 
however, increasing attention has been given to biodegradable material alternatives (Gómez & 
Escobar, 2022; Lacerda & Maceno, 2023). 
 
The scope of these studies is mostly cradle-to-grave to cover almost all impacts from the possible 
life cycle stages (Gómez & Escobar, 2022). However, exceptions are usually made regarding the 
use-phase of the studied systems due to methodological challenges and assumed negligible en-
vironmental impacts related to that stage (e.g., Birkenmaier et al., 2024; Civancik-Uslu et al., 
2019; Michaliszyn-Gabryś et al., 2022; Pitawala et al., 2022; Stafford et al., 2022; Stefanini et al., 
2024; Zuthi et al., 2025).  
 
Recent LCAs show the geographical and study-subject diversity in this research area. For in-
stance, Lacerda et al. (2024) compared HDPE, LDPE, and TPS in the Brazilian context. Pitawala 
et al. (2022) analyzed LDPE and paper bags in Sri Lanka, whereas Zuthi et al. (2025) had a 
similar interest in assessing LDPE and jute bags in Bangladesh. Also, studies with various mate-
rial options, including LDPE and starch-blends, have been conducted by Stafford et al. (2022) in 
South Africa and Civancik-Uslu et al. (2019) in Spain.  
 
 
4 In order to obtain a more comprehensive picture of sustainability, economic and social aspects must be considered. An 
LCA variation is to include these two sustainability pillars into the LCA. Therefore, the two additional categories of life 
cycle costing (LCC) for economic and social life cycle assessment (SLCA) for social aspects are analyzed (Bremenkamp 
& Gallagher, 2024; Coelho & de Souza Henriques, 2025). According to ISO14040, LCC and SLCA are not part of an LCA, 
but can be applied if beneficial. However, the combination of all three components is difficult to implement and has been 
less strongly analyzed. (Bremenkamp & Gallagher, 2024; Zira et al., 2021). 
5 Further details for on performing an LCA can be found in ISO14044 (ISO, 2021) and the ILCD handbook (Joint Research 
Centre: Institute for Environment and Sustainability, 2010) 
==PAGE:21==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
11 
Although direct comparisons between studies are difficult due to, e.g., differing functional units, 
system boundaries, impact assessment methods, and studied materials, a general pattern 
emerges: Fossil-based plastics tend to perform better in LCAs for single-use plastic bags. This is 
primarily attributed to lower material requirements. Green materials often require more material 
mass to achieve comparable functional characteristics, which results in increased resource use 
(Gómez & Escobar, 2022; Lacerda & Maceno, 2023).  
 
Bisinella et al. (2018) conducted an LCA study on different shopping bag materials, including 
LDPE and TPS, for the Danish context. However, VLPCBs were explicitly excluded from the 
study. Bisinella et al. (2018) based their decision on the European Parliament and Council Di-
rective 94/62/EC. At that time, VLPCBs were excluded from the measures to reduce packaging 
and packaging waste. 
 
To the best of current knowledge, the only known LCA specifically addressing VLPCBs has been 
conducted by the European Commission (Ballinger & Hann, 2016). This study assessed the four 
material options: (1) HDPE, (2) paper, (3) starch with polycaprolactone (bio-based and fossil-
based polymer blend) and (4) PLA for the European market. Given the scope of their study, Ball-
inger & Hann (2016) concluded that no preferrable material could be identified as none of the 
studied materials consistently outperformed the others across all the assessed impact categories.  
 
In light of this ambiguity, this study responds to the call for further research expressed in the 
European Directive 2018/852 amending the 94/62/EC, which states “Bio-based recyclable pack-
aging and compostable biodegradable packaging could represent an opportunity to promote re-
newable sources for the production of packaging, where shown to be beneficial from a life-cycle 
perspective.“ Accordingly, this study provides the first assessment of VLPCBs for food packaging 
in the Danish context, focusing on PLA and starch-based material options.  
 
3.5 Planetary Boundaries 
 
The LCA evaluates the studied system’s environmental impacts relative to the impacts of the 
potential available alternatives. In that sense, the results of LCA studies are relative (Bjørn, Chan-
drakumar, et al., 2020; Hauschild, 2015; Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual, 2024). To assess the eco-
efficiency in absolute terms, absolute limits must be defined. However, setting a reference point 
for assessing the absolute sustainability of a studied system is challenging (Sala et al., 2020). 
One established key concept for defining environmental limit reference points is the PB frame-
work, as it has been widely used in academia, business and policy guidelines (Paulillo & Sanyé-
Mengual, 2024; Rockström et al., 2024; Ryberg, Owsianiak, Clavreul, et al., 2018; Sala et al., 
2020).  
 
The PB framework, initially introduced by Rockström et al., (2009), delineates a SOS for human 
activities at a global scale. Transgressing the SOS increases the risk of destabilizing the Earth 
System (ES) which refers to the entirety of interacting biological, chemical, physical and human 
processes that enable life on the planet (Rockström et al., 2009; Steffen et al., 2007). The ES is 
modelled by nine systems and processes, also called PB processes, representing critical func-
tions that are responsible for the ES regulation. The status of the PB processes is quantitatively 
measured by control variables (CV). For each CV, boundary values limiting the SOS for the re-
spective PB process are defined. Thus, the term “safe operating space” is used interchangeably 
==PAGE:22==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
12 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
(Richardson et al., 2023; Rockström et al., 2024). To avoid ambiguity, the study uses the unam-
biguous terms and the corresponding definition according Table 3-1. Figure 3-2 and Figure 3-3 
illustrates the terms graphically.  
 
As the PB processes are interlinked, transgressing a single PB boundary may compromise the 
exceedance of others, risking the instability of the ES (Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual, 2024). There-
fore, all PBs must be respected simultaneously to achieve continuous ES stability. Although it is 
imperative that no PBs are violated, human activities have resulted in the exceedance of six PBs 
already (illustrated in Figure 3-2) (Richardson et al., 2023).

3.6 Absolute Environmental Sustainability Assessment 
 
AESA is an emerging concept for sustainability assessments and refers to the integration of the 
PB framework into the LCA methodology (Bjørn, Chandrakumar, et al., 2020; Paulillo & Sanyé-
Mengual, 2024).6 This approach enables the assessment of the environmental impacts quantified 
by LCA with the absolute limits set by the PB framework (Clausen et al., 2024; Ryberg, Bjerre, et 
al., 2020). Thereby, the AESA is put into practice by a three-step approach: (1) metric definition, 
(2) definition and allocation of SOS and (3) assessment and interpretation of results (Clausen et 
al., 2024; Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual, 2024). Each step is described in detail below.  
 
6 While other frameworks, respectively, ecological limits can serve as absolute limits in AESA studies (Bjørn, Chandra-
kumar, et al., 2020; Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual, 2024), this study focuses on the PB framework. 
==PAGE:24==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
14 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
 
Figure 3-4 - AESA Three-Step Approach including different Operationalization Possibilities 
Source: Adapted from Clausen et al. (2024) & Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual (2024) 
 
 
3.6.1 Step 1: Metric Definition 
 
First, to combine the LCA methodology and the PB framework, metrics have to be harmonized. 
This is necessary as both concepts were developed independently and thus rely on different met-
rics (Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual, 2024). Therefore, harmonized metrics enable the assessment of 
sustainability (Clausen et al., 2024). For this step, two possible approaches exist: (1) translation 
of the PBs into conventional LCA metrics or (2) translation of LCA metrics into PB framework 
metrics (Bjørn, Chandrakumar, et al., 2020; Clausen et al., 2024; Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual, 2024; 
Sala et al., 2020).  
 
Out of the two translation approaches, this study adopts the second one due to both practical and 
methodological considerations. Although different methods exist for translating PBs into LCA met-
rics (Clausen et al., 2024), the matching of PBs with LCA impact categories can be challenging 
and complex, as the links between them are often non-unique (Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual, 2024; 
Ryberg, Owsianiak, Clavreul, et al., 2018). In contrast, the PB-LCIA method, developed by 
Ryberg, Owsianiak, Richardson, et al. (2018), offers a more straightforward approach for trans-
lating LCA metrics into PB framework metrics (Bjørn, Sim, et al., 2020). This method includes 
characterization factors which are used in the LCIA phase to directly convert LCA elementary 
flows into PB metrics (Ryberg, Owsianiak, Clavreul, et al., 2018). 
 
 
==PAGE:25==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
15 
3.6.2 Step 2: Definition and allocation of SOS 
 
3.6.2.1 Step 2.1: Definition of SOS 
 
In the second step, the SOS must first be defined for the AESA study. As described in the previous 
Section, the PB framework delineates a SOS in which humans can operate safely, without desta-
bilizing the ES (Richardson et al., 2023; Rockström et al., 2009; Steffen et al., 2015). However, 
there are two different approaches for defining the SOS (Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual, 2024; Ryberg, 
Owsianiak, Clavreul, et al., 2018). The first approach defines the SOS by accounting for the share 
of SOS already occupied by nature, while the second approach is based on the status quo. 
 
In the first approach, the available SOS for humanity is calculated as the difference between the 
respective PB and the portion which is already occupied by nature, also referred to as the natural 
background level (see Section 4.3.1, Table 4-7). When applying this approach, a studied system 
is considered absolutely sustainable if its environmental impacts remain within the allocated 
SoSOS. In this condition, the system does not contribute to exceeding the total SOS leading to 
destabilizing the Earth System. Thus, the current exceedance of the PBs (as discussed in Section 
3.5) is due to other systems (Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual, 2024; Ryberg, Owsianiak, Clavreul, et 
al., 2018). In contrast, the second approach defines the SOS as the delta between the PB and 
the current value of the respective CV. This means, the status quo serves as the baseline for the 
assessment. Rather than allocating a share of the total SOS, only a portion of the so-called re-
maining SOS is allocated to the studied system. Thus, the environmental impacts are compared 
against a smaller share, leading to more restrictive assessment. This approach becomes prob-
lematic for already transgressed PBs, where the remaining SOS becomes negative. In this case, 
the studied system needs to achieve negative environmental impacts in order to be classified as 
absolutely sustainable. However, only a few technologies meet this requirement today (Paulillo & 
Sanyé-Mengual, 2024; Ryberg, Owsianiak, Clavreul, et al., 2018).  
 
As displayed, the two approaches lead to different results. The first approach enables the identi-
fication of systems that operate safely within the limits for a stable ES. The second approach 
enables the identification of systems that can contribute to restoring currently transgressed PBs 
and thereby help to return to the SOS. For instance, in the case of the PB process Climate 
Change, by producing negative carbon emissions (Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual, 2024; Ryberg, 
Owsianiak, Clavreul, et al., 2018).  
 
For the purpose of this study, the first approach is applied, as it enables a fair allocation of SoSOS 
and thus provides a more robust basis for assessing the sustainability of the studied system – 
VLPCBs. As noted by Ryberg, Owsianiak, Clavreul, et al. (2018), the second approach may dis-
favor new technologies, as they are evaluated against more strict absolute limits, rather than a 
share of the full SOS that would be theoretically available to them. As a result, systems with 
perhaps less environmental impacts could be classified as not absolutely sustainable which may 
discourage a shift towards more sustainable alternatives.7 
 
 
 
7 Also, the opposite scenario is possible. A new technology is seen as absolutely sustainable in the first approach, but not 
in the second. However, to the best of knowledge, today no study was found comparing these two approaches yet.  
==PAGE:26==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
16 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
3.6.2.2 Step 2.2: Definition SoSOS by Sharing Principles 
 
After the definition of the SOS for the AESA study, a downscaling step to the level of analysis 
must follow (Guinée et al., 2022; Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual, 2024). This is necessary because 
the PBs are defined at a global level, whereas the system under study of AESAs typically refer to 
specific actors or activities, for example countries, industries, companies, or individual products 
(Oosterhoff et al., 2023; Ryberg, Andersen, et al., 2020a). Consequently, the SOS must be ap-
portioned and assigned to the system under study, resulting in a SoSOS that serves as a refer-
ence for evaluating the environmental sustainability of that system (Bjørn, Chandrakumar, et al., 
2020; Oosterhoff et al., 2023). 
 
This allocation step, also referred to as downscaling, is inherently normative, as it requires a 
decision on how the global SOS should be distributed among different entities (Oosterhoff et al., 
2023; Ryberg, Andersen, et al., 2020a). For this purpose, various sharing principles8 have been 
used in previous AESA studies. Bjørn et al. (2020) and Ryberg et al. (2020) assessed several 
AESA studies. They found out that 34 respective 10 different sharing principles were used within 
the reviewed studies. The most used sharing principle is equal per capita (EPC) which belongs 
to the ethical norm of egalitarianism. By applying this sharing principle, the global SOS is treated 
as a finite resource which is equally distributed among the global population. Other generally 
applicable sharing principles belong to inegalitarianism such as acquired rights or grandfathering 
and utilitarianism like sharing based on final consumption expenditure (FCE) or gross value 
added. While the former principles share the SOS based on the current or historical environmental 
impacts of the studied system, the ladder is based on its utility, which is commonly approximated 
by the previously mentioned economic indicators in the context of AESA studies. Other mentioned 
principles in the reviews by Bjørn et al. (2020) and Ryberg et al. (2020) are less common in AESA 
studies, mostly because of lacking necessary data. Given the variety of sharing principles, the 
selection or the combination thereof represents a challenge. This is due to the absence of “right 
way to do it”. Rather, the underlying perspectives and priorities in each AESA study form the basis 
for choosing the sharing principles (Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual, 2024). 
 
Moreover, selecting sharing principles is not merely challenging but also represents a critical 
choice in an AESA study. As highlighted by Bjørn et al. (2020) and Ryberg et al. (2020), the choice 
of sharing principles directly influences the results of the AESA study. Furthermore, studies by 
Ryberg, Owsianiak, Clavreul, et al. (2018) and Sandin et al. (2015) have shown that this choice 
influences the results the most. Therefore, this decision is a key determinant in AESA studies. 
Despite the importance of this decision on sharing principles, there is currently no consensus for 
selection and application (Bachmann et al., 2023; Biermann & Kim, 2020; Bjørn, Chandrakumar, 
et al., 2020; Hjalsted et al., 2021; Jabot, 2022; Ryberg, Andersen, et al., 2020a). Thus, due to the 
lack of a harmonized approach for selecting the sharing principles, the outcomes of AESA studies 
may vary considerably, thereby limiting their comparability (Oosterhoff et al., 2023; Ryberg, An-
dersen, et al., 2020a).  
 
 
8 In this study the term sharing principle is used. However, the term sharing principle is used interchangeably in literature 
with other terms such as allocation, equity, or distributive principle (Hjalsted et al., 2021; Ryberg, Andersen, et al., 2020a). 
All terms refer to methodologies and decisions to scale down PBs from a global to a more granular level.  
==PAGE:27==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
17 
Nevertheless, initial efforts to harmonize the allocation step in AESA studies have already been 
made. Ryberg et al. (2020), for instance, developed a framework to guide the assignment of the 
SoSOS. This framework comprises recommendations on the selection of sharing principles and 
suggests a guideline for the documentation to enhance comparability and transparency of AESA 
studies. Similarly, Hjalsted et al. (2021) developed a framework aiming at standardizing the allo-
cation step in AESA studies focusing on industrial units. Given the strong influence of the alloca-
tion principles on AESA outcomes, Bjørn et al. (2020) recommend to apply multiple sharing prin-
ciples to assess the sensitivity of the results. In line with this, Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual (2024) 
proposed a best practice approach, recently. It includes applying several principles to capture the 
effect of different normative assumptions. To be precise, they suggest using four distinct princi-
ples that represent diverse perspectives: (i) EPC, (ii) responsibility, (iii) grandfathering (or ac-
quired rights) and (iv) ability to pay (ATP). The rationale for this selection is based on practical 
and conceptual considerations. First, EPC and grandfathering are frequently applied principles in 
AESA studies and relatively easy to apply due to availability of applied data. In contrast, the re-
sponsibility principle, allocates a smaller share to actors with higher historical environmental im-
pacts to account for past emissions, leading to divergent results. Similarly, the ATP principle as-
signs actors with greater financial means to reduce their environmental impacts a smaller SoSOS. 
 
Moreover, there is a broad agreement in literature on the general procedure for assigning the 
SoSOS to a product system. A two-step approach is commonly applied: the global SOS is first 
downscaled to a smaller level (e.g., country level) and subsequently to the studied system (see 
Figure 3-5) (Bjørn, Chandrakumar, et al., 2020; Hjalsted et al., 2021; Oosterhoff et al., 2023; 
Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual, 2024). This is because the SOS cannot be directly allocated to non-
human entities as only humans are considered as the rights holders to the SOS (Ryberg, Ander-
sen, et al., 2020b). Consequently, industrial units can only receive a SoSOS through their con-
nection to individuals. Therefore, the downscaling to the individual is considered a necessary first 
step before translating the SOS to other system levels (Hjalsted et al., 2021). 
 
 
 
Figure 3-5 - Illustration of the Two-Step Approach for allocating a SoSOS to a studied System 
Source: adapted from Hjalsted et al. (2021) and Oosterhoff et al. (2023) – own illustration 
The figure illustrates the commonly applied two-step approach for allocating a SoSOS to a non-global sys-
tem. In the context of this thesis, the SOS is first downscaled to the national level of Denmark. In the second 
step, the national SoSOS of Denmark is further downscaled to the studied system – VLPCBs.  
In general, for each downscaling step, one or multiple sharing principles can be applied. 
 
In line with the presented recommendations for improving the harmonization of AESA studies, 
this thesis adopts the two-step approach for assigning a share of safe operating space (SoSOS) 
to a studied system, as described by (Bjørn et al., 2025; Hjalsted et al., 2021; Paulillo & Sanyé-
==PAGE:28==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
18 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
Mengual, 2024; Ryberg, Andersen, et al., 2020b). Furthermore, to account for the normative in-
fluence of the allocation choices, this study applies two different approaches. For the first ap-
proach the study follows the recommendations of Hjalsted et al. (2021) and Ryberg, Andersen, 
et al. (2020b). Thereby, the SOS is first downscaled from the global to the national level using the 
EPC principle. In the second step, the national SoSOS is further downscaled to the product level 
using the FCE principle, which is, as previously mentioned commonly applied as a proxy for utility 
(Hjalsted et al., 2021; Perdomo Echenique et al., 2022). The second approach follows the recom-
mendations by Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual (2024) and applies the ATP principle for the first 
downscaling step from global to national level, followed again by downscaling to product level 
using the FCE principle. This comparison, as part of the sensitivity analysis will show the how 
different perspectives in the first downscaling step (egalitarianism (EPC) and prioritarianism 
(ATP)) influence the SoSOS and consequently the results of the AESA study. 
 
The often-used grandfathering principle is not applied in this study. Its implementation is con-
strained by the need for historical environmental impact data across all relevant PBs, which is 
either limited or entirely lacking (Bachmann et al., 2023). Furthermore, grandfathering is an ine-
galitarian principle that favors systems with historically high environmental impacts while discrim-
inating against those that are historically disadvantaged (e.g., low-and-middle income countries) 
or those that have already reduced their environmental pressures (Bachmann et al., 2023; Guinée 
et al., 2022; Hjalsted et al., 2021). Adding to this, the principle can lead to biased conclusions. 
High historical impacts can inflate the allocated SoSOS, which creates the impression that a sys-
tem operates within sustainable limits even if it exerts excessive environmental pressure. The 
contrasting concept of responsibility (allocating a smaller SoSOS to systems with higher historical 
impacts) is also not applied as it needs similar historical data (Lucas et al., 2020). Although 
Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual (2024) propose the use of climate change data as a proxy for other 
PBs, this approach is deemed insufficiently accurate. 
 
3.6.3 Step 3: Assessment and Interpretation of Results 
 
Finally, in the third step of the AESA framework, the SoSOS (calculated according to the selected 
sharing principles) is compared with the environmental impacts from the LCI. Thereby, a system 
can only be considered absolutely sustainable if its impacts remain within the allocated SoSOS 
(Hjalsted et al., 2021; Ryberg, Owsianiak, Clavreul, et al., 2018). For the assessment, the ratio of 
environmental impact and assigned SoSOS is calculated, also referred to as Environmental Sus-
tainability Ratio (ESR) (Ryberg, Bjerre, et al., 2020). If the ESR is less or equal to one, the studied 
system is considered sustainable with respect to the specific PB and the applied sharing principles 
(see Eq. (1)). Consequently, if the ratio exceeds one, the studied system is deemed not sustain-
able for the respective PB. Furthermore, in this case, the studied system is deemed not absolutely 
sustainable, as this designation requires that the ratio remains less or equal to one for all PBs 
considered in the AESA study. This ensures the compliance with the strong sustainability inter-
pretation of the PB framework, which does not allow for trade-offs between boundaries (see Sec-
tion 3.5).  
Eq. (1) 
 
𝑈𝑛𝑆𝑢𝑠𝑡𝑎𝑖𝑛𝑎𝑏𝑙𝑒= 1 ≥ 𝐸𝑛𝑣𝑖𝑟𝑜𝑛𝑚𝑒𝑛𝑡𝑎𝑙 𝐼𝑚𝑝𝑎𝑐𝑡𝑃𝐵
𝐴𝑠𝑠𝑖𝑔𝑛𝑒𝑑 𝑆𝑜𝑆𝑂𝑆𝑃𝐵

3.6.4 Current Literature on AESA Studies 
 
In contrast to conventional LCA, AESA is a young but emerging concept for sustainability assess-
ments (Bjørn et al., 2025; Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual, 2024). Bjørn, Chandrakumar, et al. (2020) 
conducted the first comprehensive review of AESA studies in their seminal work and identified 45 
relevant studies up to that point. Since then, the number of AESA-related publications has grown. 
However, AESA studies are still limited today (Andersen et al., 2024; Mahmood et al., 2023). 
 
Recent AESA studies have studied various systems across multiple scales and sectors. On a 
geographical level, studies have been conducted for major economies such as the EU (European 
Environment Agency, 2020) or Brazil, China, India, Japan, Russia and the United States (Lucas 
et al., 2020; van den Berg et al., 2020). Furthermore, studies focused on specific economic sec-
tors. These include, for instance, various sectors in New Zealand (Chandrakumar et al., 2020), 
the dairy industry in Denmark, India, and on a global level (Hjalsted et al., 2021), as well as Finnish 
agricultural products (Kyttä et al., 2025) and chemical sectors (Tulus et al., 2021).  
 
On a lower scale, AESA studies concentrated on companies (e.g., Ryberg, Bjerre, et al. (2020)), 
products and services. Product-level assessments include, for example, wood panels (Perdomo 
Echenique et al., 2022), sodium-ion and lithium-ion batteries (M. Kumar et al., 2025), electric 
vehicles (A.-R. Ali et al., 2023), urea fertilizer (Masjedi et al., 2024), rice (Mahmood et al., 2023), 
and sugarcane (Ghani et al., 2023). In terms of services, AESA studies assessed, for instance, 
laundry washing (Ryberg, Owsianiak, Clavreul, et al., 2018) or municipal solid waste management 
(Rodrigues et al., 2021). 
 
However, studies specifically addressing green materials remain scarce. Among the few publica-
tions, succinic acid and PLA (Meramo et al., 2024), biofuels (Lopes Silva et al., 2025), and alter-
native production technologies and feedstocks including biomass for plastics (Bachmann, 2024) 
and BTX chemicals (Zuiderveen et al., 2024) were assessed.  
 
Notably, the findings by Tulus et al. (2021) revealed that more than 99% of the assessed chemical 
products exceeded their assigned SoSOS and are thus not considered absolutely sustainable. At 
the same time, as previously discussed, the terms “biobased” and “biodegradable” are insufficient 
indicators for environmental sustainability, as the substitution of fossil-based with green materials 
can lead to burden shifting, e.g., due to increased land use and water consumption (Bachmann 
et al., 2023). However, these impacts are rarely evaluated against the planetary boundaries in 
academia today.  
 
To the best of current knowledge, no AESA study has yet been published on food packaging. The 
study fills this gap by providing the first AESA study on VLPCBs. In doing so, the study responds 
to recent calls in literature (e.g., Bachmann et al., 2023) to assess whether replacing fossil-based 
materials with green alternatives constitutes a meaningful option or a drawback for achieving 
absolute environmental sustainability in food packaging.

TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK

Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
4. Material and Methods 
This chapter presents the methodological steps applied in this study to obtain the results. The 
study combined the steps of the LCA and AESA framework as outlined in the previous chapter. 
Figure 4-1 presents the methodological flowchart that guided the AESA of VLPCBs in the Danish 
context.  
 
 
Figure 4-1 - Methodology Flowchart 
Source: own illustration 
 
 
==PAGE:31==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
21 
4.1 Goal and Scope Definition 
 
4.1.1 Goal of the Study 
 
The goal of the study is to conduct a comparative LCA of VLPCBs used for short-term transpor-
tation of fresh fruits and vegetables from retailers to consumer households in Denmark. The study 
compares the environmental impacts of VLPCBs made from conventional fossil-based plastic 
(LDPE) with those made from two alternative green materials: (1) PLA and (2) starch, as these 
materials represent promising alternatives to conventional plastics in the food packaging context 
(Cruz et al., 2022; García-Guzmán et al., 2022; Nian et al., 2024; Swetha et al., 2023).  
 
This assessment investigates whether VLPCBs made from green materials offer an environmen-
tally superior alternative to conventional LDPE-based VLPCBs. This is done by performing an 
AESA by linking the results to the PB framework (see Section 3.5 & 3.6) following the PB-LCIA 
approach proposed by Ryberg, Owsianiak, Richardson, et al. (2018). The study results show if 
the studied materials remain within the assigned SoSOS. 
 
To the best of the research conducted for this study, only one LCA has been identified that ex-
plicitly focuses on VLPCBs. However, most existing studies examine LPCBs or other kinds of 
bags for shopping purposes with a thickness greater than 15 microns. Moreover, no peer-re-
viewed study has conducted an AESA for VLPCBs. Therefore, the study addresses this gap in 
the literature by providing the first AESA of VLPCBs in this research field.  
 
The study follows the standardized methodology defined by ISO 14040 (ISO, 2006) and ISO 
14044 (ISO, 2021) and applies the detailed guidance of the International Reference Life Cycle 
Data System (ILCD) Handbook (Joint Research Centre: Institute for Environment and Sustaina-
bility, 2010). The study is conducted as a master’s thesis at the Technical University of Denmark, 
at the Department of Engineering Technology. The results are intended primarily for academic 
and research purposes. Therefore, the study is only of descriptive nature and corresponds ac-
cording to the ILCD Handbook to study type C1. By this definition, the study is an accounting 
assessment without impact on political or market-related decisions. Consequently, following the 
ILCD handbook guidelines, an attributional modeling approach was used in this study to represent 
the life cycle stages of VLPCBs used in Denmark in 2022 as accurately as possible.  
 
The study adopts a cradle-to-grave system perspective, covering all relevant life cycle steps from 
raw material extraction to end-of-life treatment. The LCI modeling was performed using the LCA 
software OpenLCA v2.4.1 (GreenDelta GmbH, 2025b) based on data from ecoinvent v3.11 
(APOS) (Wernet et al., 2016). No primary data from VLPCB manufacturers, logistics companies 
or waste treatment facilities was collected. Data from previous studies was included when appro-
priate. The study focuses on market-relevant and widely used material types to select representa-
tive green materials of the previously mentioned categories. For PLA, the study refers to material 
produced by NatureWorks, one of the leading PLA producers worldwide (HDIN Research, 2023; 
Markets and Markets, 2023). Regarding starch-based material, the study focuses on TPS. Ac-
cording to Werther et al. (2023), TPS and PLA represent the most prevalent green material types 
on the Danish market for flexible food packaging. Their study further reveals that TPS-based 
products are frequently made of Mater-Bi, which is a commercial TPS raw material produced by 
==PAGE:32==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
22 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
Novamont, a pioneer in biobased and biodegradable plastics (Bastioli, 1998). This finding is con-
sistent with recent market research, which identifies Novamont as a dominant market leader for 
TPS materials (Mordor Intelligence, 2025b), accounting for approximately 41% of the global TPS 
market (Verified Market Reports, 2025). However, Mater-Bi is available in different blends from 
25-100% biobased content (Hann, Scholes, Lee, et al., 2020). The dataset that represents Mater-
Bi in ecoinvent refers to a TPS blend with 35% biobased and 65% fossil-based content (ecoinvent, 
2025f). In order to assess a fully biobased TPS alternative, a hypothetical TPS material compo-
sition was modeled (see Section 4.2.2)9. To distinguish between the two TPS alternatives, the 
TPS blend with fossil-based components is hereafter called TPS1, while the fully bio-based alter-
native is called TPS2.  
 
4.1.2 Scope of the Study 
 
4.1.2.1 Functional Unit 
 
The functional unit (FU) is a key element of an LCA, as the relative assessment of the studied 
system is structured around it (ISO, 2006). The FU must be clearly quantitatively and qualitatively 
defined to enable a meaningful comparison of the different materials potentially used for VLPCBs 
in Denmark (European Commission. Joint Research Centre. Institute for Environment and Sus-
tainability., 2010). 
 
The primary purpose of the VLPCBs is to assist in transporting fresh fruits and vegetables from 
supermarkets to consumer households (Civancik-Uslu et al., 2019). Thereby, the VLPCBs protect 
the fresh produce from external influences such as humidity or microbial contamination, among 
others, to maintain high food quality until the products are unpacked at home (Gaspar & Braga, 
2023; González-López et al., 2023; Khalid & Arif, 2022). To ensure the protective function, the 
materials for VLPCBs must be food-safe. The materials selected for this study fulfill this require-
ment (see Section 3.3) and are already used in the Danish market (see Section 4.2.1). 
 
According to the PB-LCIA framework, the FU must be scaled to a full year (Ryberg, Owsianiak, 
Richardson, et al., 2018). For this study, the annual consumption of single-use VLPCBs in Den-
mark in 2022 will therefore serve as the reference for the definition of the FU. Denmark is chosen 
as the country of interest since the study is conducted as a master’s thesis at the Technical Uni-
versity of Denmark. Furthermore, this geographical boundary serves as the reference for 
downscaling and allocating the PBs (see Section 4.4). 
 
The temporal scope 2022 is chosen as a reference year as it provides Denmark’s most recent 
and complete data on per capita consumption of VLPCBs available from Eurostat (2024), report-
ing a national average of 19.6 LPCBs per capita. Since the statistics for Denmark do not distin-
guish between LPCBs and VLPCBs, the share of VLPCBs is estimated using the mean proportion 
of VLPCBs reported by EU Member states with disaggregated data. The mean average is 67.54% 
(see Appendix A-1). Based on this assumption, the estimated per capita consumption of VLPCBs 
in Denmark in 2022 is calculated by multiplying the reported average of 19.6 LPCBs by the 
VLPCB share of 67.54%, resulting in approximately 13.24 VLPCBs per person. Multiplying this 
 
9 A hypothetical case of a fully bio-based PLA is not necessary, as NatureWorks products are 100% biobased 
(NatureWorks, 2025) which is reflected in the corresponding ecoinvent v3.11 (APOS) dataset for PLA (ecoinvent, 2025). 
==PAGE:33==

23 
figure by the reported Danish population in 2022 (5,934,002 inhabitants (Statistics Denmark, 
2025)) yields an estimated total annual consumption of 78,566,187 VLPCBs.  
 
Furthermore, legislation must also be considered. According to the EU directive, the classes of 
carrier bags are defined by wall thickness. In terms of VLPCBs the wall thickness is limited to 15 
microns (Directive (EU) 2015/720 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 29 April 2015 
Amending Directive 94/62/EC as Regards Reducing the Consumption of Lightweight Plastic Car-
rier Bags (Text with EEA Relevance), 2015). Based on the presented information, the FU for this 
study is therefore defined as: 
 
The consumption of 78,566,187 single-use VLPCBs with the format 400mm x 200mm a wall thick-
ness of 15µm in Denmark in 2022.10  
 
The corresponding reference flow, which represents the material specific mass required to fulfil 
the FU, is calculated based on the material density and bag dimensions in the following Section.  
 
4.1.2.2 Reference Flow 
 
To enable a material-specific LCI, the reference flow (RF) for each VLPCB material was calcu-
lated. The reference flow refers to the specific mass of each VLPCB material required to fulfill the 
FU defined for this study.  
 
It is important to note that different materials have different mechanical properties, so their ability 
to withstand stress may influence the number of VLPCBs needed in practice. Therefore, varying 
carrying capacities of VLPCBs made of different materials could affect their ability to fulfil the FU. 
However, the carrying capacity is also influenced by the properties of the transported goods. 
Therefore, volume and weight have also an impact on the VLPCBs. As a result, in cases where 
VLPCBs tear more easily due to their material, more VLPCBs are needed to fulfil the FU, as they 
cannot be filled to the same capacity (United Nations Environment Program, 2020). 
 
To evaluate this potential effect, tensile strength values of the studied materials were compared 
(see Table 4-1). In this context, the tensile strength reflects the force a film can withstand before 
tearing (Michelot et al., 2017). LDPE and TPS show comparable properties, while PLA exceeds 
 
10 Since VLPCBs are produced and offered in various sizes and wall thicknesses (Ballinger & Hann, 2016), the maximum 
permissible wall thickness of 15 µm, as defined by the EU Directive 2015/720/EU (Directive (EU) 2015/720 of the Euro-
pean Parliament and of the Council of 29 April 2015 Amending Directive 94/62/EC as Regards Reducing the Consumption 
of Lightweight Plastic Carrier Bags (Text with EEA Relevance), 2015), was chosen for the definition of the FU. This rep-
resents a conservative assumption, as selecting the maximum thickness accounts for increased material use, which is 
assumed to lead to higher environmental impacts per unit and thus enables a more stringent assessment. The dimensions 
of the representative VLPCB used for the FU are based on a systematic analysis of product listings from Danish retailers 
(as of 30 May 2025; see Appendix A-2). A total of 100 offers were reviewed. The analysis revealed that Danish suppliers 
exclusively offer LDPE plastic bags for food applications within the category of LPCBs (wall thickness 15 < x ≤ 50 µm). No 
offers of LDPE VLPCBs intended and safe for food use were identified. Therefore, the dimensions of typical LPCBs were 
used as a proxy and transferred to the VLPCB for FU modeling. The most frequently offered dimensions were 
400 × 200 mm. As wall thickness influences the overall material required (Ballinger & Hann, 2016), a reduced wall thick-
ness will be assessed as part of the sensitivity analysis.

Furthermore, the study assumes that all VPLCBs, regardless of the material, are filled to the same 
extent. This assumption is based on two factors: (1) using a standardized VLPCB format with 
identical volumes across all materials and (2) the similar tensile strength of the selected materials, 
which implies equivalent carrying capacity.  
 
In contrast to other studies that assess shopping bags of different formats (e.g., varying volumes) 
or mechanical properties, this study must not adjust for partial usage. This is further justified by 
the definition of the FU on actual Danish consumption data for VLPCBs in 2022. Any real-world 
inefficiencies of e.g., underfilling or additional use due to damage are already reflected in the 
reported consumption figure. Therefore, no further adjustments in this regard are necessary.

applied to all materials: It is assumed that VLPCBs are produced by combining two plastic film 
sheets with the dimensions defined in the FU.
