4.1.2.3 System Boundaries 
 
The study applies a cradle-to-grave system perspective as defined in Section 4.1.1. Accordingly, 
the system boundaries include all relevant life cycle stages, from raw material extraction to end-
of-life treatment to perform the AESA study as comprehensively as possible. In line with existing 
LCA studies on carrier bags, the use-phase is excluded (Birkenmaier et al., 2024; Civancik-Uslu 
et al., 2019; Michaliszyn-Gabryś et al., 2022; Pitawala et al., 2022; Stafford et al., 2022; Stefanini 
et al., 2024; Zuthi et al., 2025). Figure 4-3 displays the system boundary and associated life cycle 
stages, further detailed in Section 4.2. In the following, each life cycle stage and the corresponding 
processes as well as exclusions are described in accordance with ISO14044 (ISO, 2021).  
 
 
12 According to the Advancing Standards Transforming Markets (ASTM) standard specification D4976-12a, the density 
range for LDPE is between 0.910 – 0.925 g/cm3 (ASTM International, 2020b). 
==PAGE:36==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
26 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
 
 
Figure 4-3 - LCA System Boundaries 
Source: own illustration 
 
Raw Material Extraction and Preprocessing 
Figure 4-3 shows that “Raw Material Extraction and Preprocessing” constitute the first life cycle 
stages. The respective raw material inputs differ by material type and are therefore modeled ac-
cordingly in the LCI (see Section 4.2.2): LDPE is fossil-based, while PLA and TPS2 are biobased. 
TPS1 represents a blend of fossil and bio-based sources. 
 
For each material, the life cycle stage of “Raw Material extraction and Preprocessing” includes 
the following processes: 
 
(1) Extraction or harvesting of the primary raw material (e.g., natural gas for LDPE (Avery et 
al., 2025; ecoinvent, 2025e), maize grain for PLA13 (Swetha et al., 2023)) and TPS2, both 
for TPS114). 
 
13 NatureWorks uses maize as the raw material for producing PLA (ecoinvent, 2025h; Vink et al., 2003). 
14 Novamont uses both maize and natural gas as raw material for the Mater-Bi product (TPS1) (ecoinvent, 2025f) 
==PAGE:37==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
27 
(2) Conversion of the feedstock into granulates (used as raw materials for the subsequent 
VLPCB manufacturing) 
(3) Transport of the converted product to its consumer, i.e., the VLPCB manufacturing facil-
ities 
 
Furthermore, all upstream energy, resources, and auxiliary inputs supply, process emissions, and 
waste flows are included for each material. Energy and resource requirements for the production, 
maintenance, or decommissioning of capital goods such as machinery or industrial plants re-
quired for the aforementioned processes are excluded in this phase, as these are not included in 
the used ecoinvent data to model this life cycle stage. 
 
VLPCB Manufacturing 
This life cycle stage represents converting raw materials (granulates) into VLPCBs. In line with 
previous LCA studies, the manufacturing process is modeled using film extrusion as the repre-
sentative technology (e.g., (Askham et al., 2021; Zuthi et al., 2025)). Similar to the previous life 
cycle stage, all relevant inputs regarding the VLPCB manufacturing, such as heat and energy 
demands, auxiliary materials, and generated waste and elementary flows are accounted for within 
the system boundaries. In contrast, all inputs regarding capital goods’ construction, maintenance, 
and decommissioning remain excluded. The life cycle stage ends with the manufactured VLPCBs 
at the factory gate.  
 
The study models both life cycle stages as an integrated cradle-to-gate approach as the used 
ecoinvent data connects the respective unit processes of the life cycle stages within the supply 
chain by market activities. The respective production of auxiliary materials and other supplies and 
the associated transportation of these products must not be modeled separately. For more infor-
mation see Section 4.2.2 and 4.2.3. 
 
Since VLPCBs used in Denmark are mainly imported, the study defined specific countries as key 
VLPCB suppliers of Denmark. These countries were identified based on Danish trade data sta-
tistics (see Section 4.2.1.2). Besides domestic production in Denmark, the study accounts for 
imports from other European countries (i.e., Estonia and Germany) as well as from Asia (i.e., 
China and Vietnam). To account for the geographic origin of raw materials, trade data of the 
exporting countries was analyzed to enable a more accurate modeling of the upstream supply 
chains. 
 
Transportation and Distribution 
Since the production of the VLPCBs occurs globally, the system boundaries include the logistics 
related to the VLPCBs. Modeling of the transport system includes: (1) international transport from 
VLPCB manufacturing sites to Denmark per road and maritime freight, (2) domestic distribution 
to the five Danish regions per road freight, and (3) collection of post-consumer VLPCB waste and 
transportation to waste treatment facilities. These transport processes considered in this study 
provide the service of physically relocating goods and waste without inducing any transformation 
to the VLPCBs. For further details, see Section 4.2.3. 
 
In contrast to the previously described production-related life cycle stages, all flows related to the 
respective transport life cycle stages are included. This means that in addition to direct fuel and 
==PAGE:38==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
28 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
energy inputs (e.g., diesel for lorries and heavy fuel for maritime freight) as well as associated 
emissions, the system boundaries also encompass the following elements: 
 
(1) Construction, maintenance, operation, and EoL treatment of vehicles and ships 
(2) Construction, maintenance, operation, and decommissioning of related infrastructure, 
e.g., roads and ports 
 
In this context, the inclusion of flows connected to capital goods in this context results in a mod-
eling inconsistency across the different life cycle stages. This inconsistency does not result from 
methodological preference but reflects the scope and content of the datasets provided by ecoin-
vent database used in this study. It will be addressed and discussed in the limitations section.  
 
VLPCB End-of-Life Treatment 
The EoL stage comprises the treatment of VLPCB waste after its use phase. Before EoL treat-
ment, all VLPCB waste is assumed to undergo a sorting process separating the collected post-
consumer VLPCBs into fractions for subsequent treatment. Therefore, the sorting process is in-
cluded within the system boundaries. The end-of-life treatment options depend on the material 
type and reflect current Danish waste management practices. For LDPE, EoL options include 
recycling and incineration with energy recovery. The options for PLA, TPS1 and TPS2 include 
industrial composting and incineration with energy recovery. For more details see Section 4.2.1.3.  
 
While the used ecoinvent dataset for LDPE waste treatment accounts for the upstream waste 
sorting processes, no such datasets were available for PLA, TPS1 and TPS2. Therefore, the 
sorting processes were modeled separately for these materials. The required input for the sorting 
activities is based on energy and heat requirements reported for a Danish sorting facility (Bisinella 
et al., 2018). The Danish electricity market available in ecoinvent was considered for the electricity 
supply, which includes all upstream and downstream flows associated with electricity production. 
The heat demand was modeled using the national Danish heat supply composition of 2022 pub-
lished by the Danish Energy Agency (2024)(see also Appendix A-3). However, capital goods are 
accounted for. 
 
All EoL treatment options result in the generation of by-products with economic value. These are 
electricity from incineration, biogas from industrial composting and recycled material from LDPE 
recycling. As the study applies the APOS system model, the environmental burdens of these 
processes are allocated between the treated waste material and the resulting by-product (Wernet 
et al., 2016). For instance, the environmental impacts of LDPE recycling are partially allocated to 
the upstream VLPCB product system, and partially to the downstream consumer of the recycled 
LDPE. Because these by-products enter other product systems outside the studied VLPCB life 
cycle, they are considered outside the system boundaries. Therefore, they are visually repre-
sented as greyed out in Figure 4-3. An exception applies to the generated electricity from incin-
eration. According to the ecoinvent dataset, the resulting electricity is reused internally within the 
incineration facility (ecoinvent, 2025k). Therefore, although it is part of the output flows, the elec-
tricity reused remains within the system boundaries but is visually treated similarly for consistency. 
All modeled waste treatment processes account for energy and water use, capital goods and 
infrastructure and elementary and waste flows.  
 
 
==PAGE:39==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
29 
Excluded Processes 
Based on considerations of data availability and quality, methodological alignment with previous 
LCA studies, and the expected impact on overall results, the study excluded the following life 
cycle stages and associated processes:  
 
(1) the distribution of VLPCBs from regional logistic hubs to supermarkets and  
(2) use-phase, including potential reuse and repurposing. 
 
First, the exclusion of transport activities between regional distribution hubs and supermarkets 
within Denmark is based on the heterogenous and widely dispersed nature of the supermarkets. 
As such, deriving representative average transport distances was considered very complex and 
therefore infeasible within the study’s scope. In line with previous studies (e.g., Lacerda et al. 
(2024)), this transport stage is excluded assuming the transport activities are identical and the 
associated environmental impacts are similar across the assessed VLPCB materials. 
 
Second, as described at the beginning of this chapter, the use-phase of the VLPCBs is excluded. 
However, it should be noted, that the use-phase includes also transport activities from supermar-
kets to households, besides the functional use of the VLPCBs (i.e., protecting fresh fruits and 
vegetables external influences). The transport activities are excluded due to a lack of robust and 
specific data of household transport behavior. While data on average distances from households 
to supermarkets is available (StatBank Denmark, 2024), the associated transport modes can vary 
widely. Consumers’ transport preferences could range from walking and cycling to public trans-
portation and private vehicles (e.g., cars, motorcycles, scooters, etc.). Assigning representative 
shares among the potentially used transport modes was deemed too speculative and unreliable 
(Stefanini et al., 2024). Therefore, these use-phase related transport activities were therefore 
omitted. 
 
Another omitted aspect related to the use-phase is reuse and repurposing of VLPCBs. Unlike 
previous LCA studies that assessed thicker bags, which may be reused (using a bag multiple 
times for shopping) or repurposed (e.g., as household waste liners), this study focuses solely on 
the single-use application of VLPCBs. This decision stems from the assumption that VLPCBs are, 
due to their wall thickness of ≤ 15µm, prone to rupture and too fragile to withstand repeated use 
or repurposing (Cornago et al., 2021). Moreover, in contrast to thicker shopping bags, data or 
estimates regarding the reuse respectively repurposing rates of VLPCBs are currently unavaila-
ble. 
 
While the study acknowledges that excluded process contribute to the total environmental bur-
den of the product system and therefore influence the results of the AESA study, their exclusion 
is considered justified for two reasons:  
 
(1) The assumed minimal contribution of these processes compared to other life cycle 
stages (Civancik-Uslu et al., 2019), and 
(2) Their uniform exclusion across all material types ensures comparability of the results, 
these choices favor no material type. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:40==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
30 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
4.1.2.4 Data Sources and Software 
 
The study used solely the ecoinvent v3.11(APOS) database (Wernet et al., 2016) for the modeling 
of the LCI. As the project goal did not include extensive data collection, no primary data from 
VLPCB manufacturers, logistics companies or waste treatment facilities was collected. Instead, 
data from previous studies were included when appropriate. All sources are transparently cited 
throughout the report. The use of ecoinvent v3.11 (APOS) ensures that the LCI reflects contem-
porary technological conditions, given that it is the most recent version available at the time of 
modeling (ecoinvent, 2025b).  
 
The LCI modeling approach aims to reflect the Danish context as closely as possible. Therefore, 
the state of the Danish market for VLPCBs was investigated in detail (see Section 4.2.1). This 
included analysis of production and trade volumes, the share of domestic versus imported 
VLPCBs, transportation modes and routes, and EoL treatment pathways for the different material 
alternatives. Where scientific or statistical data was lacking, assumptions were made. The as-
sumptions for each life cycle stage (shown in Figure 4-3) are transparently documented in Section 
4.2. 
 
The LCI modeling was conducted using openLCA v2.4.1 (GreenDelta GmbH, 2025b). For the 
LCIA, an adapted version of the dataset PB-LCIA2.03_OpenLCA (Nørrekær Lund et al., 2023), 
initially developed by Nørrekær Lund, Schjerbeck, and Winther Schor, was applied. Their dataset 
is based on the original PB-LCIA dataset developed for SimaPro by Ryberg et al. (2020). 
Nørrekær Lund, Schjerbeck, and Winther Schor initially converted the SimaPro dataset to be 
usable in openLCA v1.11 and openLCA 2.0 beta (Nørrekær Lund et al., 2023). However, after a 
thorough analysis of their PB-LCIA dataset, following a direct consultation with the developers, 
substantial modifications were required to ensure compatibility with the current versions of 
openLCA and the ecoinvent database. Details on the adaption are provided in Section 4.3.2 and 
Appendix A-4.  
 
4.2 Life Cycle Inventory Analysis 
 
Within the LCI, all relevant inputs and outputs related to the life cycle stages of the product and 
the defined scope are modeled according to ISO14040 (ISO, 2006). Following ISO 14044 (ISO, 
2021), the following subsections present the modeling of each life cycle stage. The detailed 
presentation ensures that the LCI is transparent and enables others to replicate it. The LCI 
presentation explains custom flows, adapted processes, and the respective sources. 
 
As AESA studies typically follow an attributional approach, since a SoSOS needs to be allocated 
(Bjørn et al., 2025; Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual, 2024), this study adopts an attributional modeling 
approach outlined by the ILCD handbook to represent the life cycle stages of VLPCBs used in 
Denmark in 2022 as accurately as possible (Joint Research Centre: Institute for Environment and 
Sustainability, 2010). To enable representative and context-specific modeling, the status of the 
Danish VLPCB market in 2022 was first investigated. This includes identifying relevant production 
and trade patterns, domestic versus imported bag shares, transportation modes and routes, and 
the EoL treatment pathways for different material alternatives. The subsequent section presents 
the findings of this assessment. 
 
==PAGE:41==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
31 
4.2.1 Assessment of VLPCBs in Denmark 
 
4.2.1.1 General Information 
 
Data regarding VLPCBs on the Danish market is limited. This may be due to the fact that, accord-
ing to the EU directive (Directive (EU) 2015/720 of the European Parliament and of the Council 
of 29 April 2015 Amending Directive 94/62/EC as Regards Reducing the Consumption of Light-
weight Plastic Carrier Bags (Text with EEA Relevance), 2015) member states must only report 
consumption numbers for LPCBs.15 Detailed data regarding VPLCBs are not mandatory and 
therefore not reported by every country. This applies also for Denmark (see eurostat (2024) and 
Appendix A-1).  
 
However, a 2019 study from the Danish Environmental Protection Agency confirmed the pres-
ence of VLPCBs in 2017 (Miljøstyrelsen, 2019). According to this study, interviews with selected 
Danish manufacturers and retailers revealed high variable estimations. The industry representa-
tives reported that VLPCBs accounted for approximately 1% of the total plastic bag consump-
tion.16 In contrast, interviewed representatives from supermarket chains reported significantly 
higher shares in their responses, leading the study to conclude that around 630 million VLPCBs 
were on the Danish market in 2017 (Miljøstyrelsen, 2019). Today, no more recent data is availa-
ble. 
 
Therefore, for this study, the estimated consumptions of VLPCBs in Denmark in 2022 is derived 
based on the European average as outlined in Section 4.1.2. This assumption is considered plau-
sible, given the overall decline in plastic bag consumption (across the EU) due to the regulatory 
intervention (see eurostat (2024). Consequently, the assumed Danish consumption value of 
78,566,187 VLPCBs in the year 2022 aligns with the observed trend. 
 
According to Miljøsyrelsen (2019) and Plastindustrien (2025), the VLPCBs used in Denmark are 
primarily made of LDPE. This information was confirmed by a representative of the industry as-
sociation “Plastindustrien – Branheforening for danske plastvirksomheder”, as well as by a repre-
sentative of the Danish Ministry of Environment and Food (Miljø- og Fødevareministeriet) (see 
Appendix A-13). Inquiries to Danish supermarket chains remained unanswered. 
 
Data on VLPCBs made from green materials is also scarce. No official statistics currently exist 
for the market share in Denmark. In general, statistics for the consumption of biobased and/or 
biodegradable products in Denmark are not available yet.17 However, it is estimated that biode-
gradable plastics represent less than 1% of the total plastic consumption in Denmark.18 So far, 
 
15 The Danish Ministry of Environment and Food representative confirmed that the reporting of VLPCBs has been volun-
tary. However, future endeavours will be made to collect data for VLPCBs (see Appendix A-13).  
16 This view is in line with the desk research done for this study, analyzing the offers of food-safe LDPE bags of Danish 
retailers. As mentioned in the previous chapter, the analysis revealed that Danish suppliers exclusively offer LDPE plastic 
bags for food applications within the category of LPCBs (wall thickness 15 < x ≤ 50 µm). No Danish offers of LDPE VLPCBs 
intended and safe for food use were identified. 
17 This is in line with the desk research done for this study. Statbank Denmark does not list statistics for this product 
category. 
18 This can also be attributed to the fact that, as of 2022, the global bioplastic production amounted to 312 thousand metric 
tonnes, representing 0.078% of the total global plastic production of approximately 400 million metric tons (Statista, 2025).  
==PAGE:42==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
32 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
Danish companies focused primarily on PLA and starch-blends when working with green materi-
als (Werther et al., 2023). 
 
4.2.1.2 Trade Data 
 
Detailed information on the distribution and trade flows of VLPCBs in Denmark is not available. 
This is due to the fact, that manufacturers and importers respectively trading companies are not 
obligated to report this information. However, most of the Danish plastic bags and sacks which 
include VLPCBS are primarily imported from abroad (Miljøstyrelsen, 2019). This finding is con-
sistent with the trade statistics published by Statbank Denmark for the year 2022. Following the 
methodology used by Danish Environmental Protection Agency for estimating the LPCB con-
sumption for the EU reporting (Miljøstyrelsen, 2019), this study uses the Statbank dataset “Im-
ports and Exports CN (EU Combined Nomenclature)” for the unit “39232100 Sacks and bags, 
incl. Cones of polymers of ethylene” to estimate the trade flows of LDPE-based VLPCBs in Den-
mark. Although this unit encompasses a broader product category, it represents the most detailed 
available proxy for VLPCBs made of LDPE.  
 
This statistic shows, that 98.55% of LDPE based plastic bags on the Danish market were im-
ported. Nearly 70% of these imports originated from European and 30% from Asian countries. 
The largest exporters of LDPE based VLPCBs to Denmark are Germany and Vietnam (see Figure 
4-4 and Figure 4-5 and Appendix A-5). The remaining 1.45% of VLPCBs are estimated to origi-
nate from domestic production.  
 
Analogous to the Danish Environmental Agency report for the EU reporting, the following equation 
was applied to estimate the share of domestic production (Miljøstyrelsen, 2019). 
 
Eq. (2)

production share. For this calculation, the data on the domestic production from the corresponding

[222211000]) was used. According to the PRODCOM and Statbank Denmark datasets, the fol-

As previously mentioned, minimal data available on VLPCBs made of green materials. However, 
according to Werther et al. (2023) it can be assumed that BioBag, a Norwegian company, is the 
largest exporter of bio-film-based products to Denmark. Another relevant player, mentioned by 
Werther et al. (2023), is Plant2Plast, a Danish producer and trading company. 
 
BioBag states on its website that it offers compostable carrier bags for fruits and vegetables (Bi-
oBag, 2025a). Although their product descriptions do not explicitly indicate whether their raw ma-
terial is 100% biobased, it is assumed in this study that the company utilizes 100% bio-based raw 
materials for its production. This assumption is based on further information BioBag provides. The 
company states that they use Mater-Bi as raw material (BioBag, 2025b), a biobased and biode-
gradable plastic resource produced by Novamont, a pioneer in biobased and biodegradable plas-
tics (Bastioli, 1998). However, Mater-Bi is available in different blends from 25-100% biobased 
content (Hann, Scholes, Lee, et al., 2020). For this study, the assumption is made that BioBag 
utilizes the 35% biobased variant. Furthermore, the company states that its production facility is 
in Europe, on the island of Hiiuuma, part of Estonia (BioBag, 2025b). 
 
The second company identified by Werther et al. (2023), Plant2Plast, is offering packaging prod-
ucts made from raw materials as alternatives to conventional plastics. A review of its website also 
confirms that Plant offers biobased bags (Plant2Plast, 2025b), which are primarily produced in 
China and shipped directly to customers from there (Plant2Plast, 2025a). 
 
To derive a trade flow model from this information for VLPCBs made of green materials, the study 
uses data from the Statbank Denmark dataset “Imports and Exports CN (EU Combined Nomen-
clature)” for product unit 39232990 (Sacks and bags, incl. cones, of plastics (excl. those of poly-
vinyl chloride and polymers of ethylene)). Although this unit covers a broader category of plastic 
bags made from other plastic materials, excluding polyvinyl chloride and polymers of ethylene, it 
is the most suitable proxy for estimating trade flows of biobased and biodegradable carrier bags. 
 
Both China and Estonia rank among the top 10 exporting countries of products under this category 
to Denmark (see Appendix A-6). Although they do not place in the first and second positions, they 
are considered the most relevant origin countries for VLPCBs made of green materials in this 
study, as both companies, BioBag and Plant2Plast, manufacture in these countries. No additional 
==PAGE:45==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
35 
data could be identified for other companies located in the top 10 countries that are applicable in 
the context of VLPCBs made from green materials for the Danish market. 
 
Consequently, for the purpose of this study, China and Estonia are assumed to be the only origin 
countries for imported VLPCBs made of green materials. According to Statbank Denmark, these 
two countries exported a total of 2,250,452 kg of the relevant product category to Denmark in 
2022. Of this total, China accounted for 51.80% and Estonia for 48.20%. 
 
Following the same methodological logic used for LDPE-based VLPCBs, this geographic alloca-
tion also serves as a proxy for regional representation: China represents all imports from Asia, 
and Estonia represents all imports from Europe. 
 
This approach aligns with regional production capacities for bioplastics (Statista, 2025), where 
Asia leads globally, followed by North America and Europe. However, North American countries 
accounted for only 0.4% of imports in the relevant CN category to Denmark and are thus excluded 
from further consideration (see Appendix A-6). 
 
4.2.1.3 Waste Management 
 
Waste treatment options available in Denmark include preparation for reuse, recycling, incinera-
tion, landfilling, special treatment or other final material recovery, and temporary storage 
(Miljøstyrelsen, 2024). However, according to Eurostat (2025) (see also Appendix A-7), Denmark 
only uses incineration with energy recovery and material recycling as current treatment pathways 
for plastic packaging waste, which includes VLPCBs. For the year 2022, Eurostat (2025) reports 
a recovery rate of 100% for plastic packaging in Denmark, indicating that the entirety of generated 
plastic packaging waste was treated according to the aforementioned waste treatment options 
(Eurostat, 2025).  
 
This high performance can be attributed to Denmark’s mandatory collection system for recycla-
bles. This scheme was introduced in 2021 and aims at full implementation across households 
and businesses by the end of 2025. This collection system requires waste to be separated in into 
the following fractions: cardboard, food, food- and drink cartons, hazardous waste, metal, plastics, 
residual waste and textiles. These fractions are collected through door-to-door collection or 
through collection facilities located within walking distance of the residential areas (European En-
vironment Agency, 2025). Plastic packaging that is not sorted, for example, when disposed of in 
public bins without separate waste collection bins, is typically incinerated (Werther et al., 2023). 
 
However, despite the collection system, an estimated 9% of plastic packaging waste is not cor-
rectly sorted and remains in the residual household waste, which is sent to incineration 
(Miljøstyrelsen, 2024). This residual waste is usually incinerated in combined heat and power 
(CHP) plants, which are typically owned by municipal waste management companies or munici-
palities themselves (Hann, Scholes, Tee, et al., 2020). 
 
For biodegradable plastics, the situation is different. Most biodegradable plastic products in Den-
mark are sorted as waste for incineration. This practice is reinforced by municipal guidance, as 
the majority of municipalities explicitly advise their citizens to dispose biodegradable plastics in 
==PAGE:46==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
36 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
residual waste streams. This approach reflects a systemic limitation since the Danish waste man-
agement system is not yet suitable for treating biodegradable plastic products. On the one hand, 
the quantities of biodegradable plastic waste are too low to be attractive for the recycling market 
and, therefore, to justify separate handling. On the other hand, required waste treatment upstream 
infrastructure, such as separate collection and sorting systems for biodegradable plastics, is ei-
ther missing or insufficiently developed. Furthermore, composting is currently not a component of 
the Danish waste management system and is only a marginal factor in food waste treatment 
(Werther et al., 2023).  
 
Nevertheless, biodegradable bags are already used for food waste collection within certain mu-
nicipalities. These bags are either sorted out as rejects for incineration or separated for further 
processing during food waste pre-treatment. The separated portion is then sent to the only exist-
ing industrial composting plant in Denmark, BioVækst, which agreed to compost biodegradable 
bags. Estimates for 2023 suggest that approximately 50% of biodegradable bags were inciner-
ated, while the remaining 50% were composted (Werther et al., 2023).  
 
Multiple stakeholders emphasize that if the market share of biodegradable plastics should in-
crease in future, separate collection and treatment efforts should be evaluated (Werther et al., 
2023). In contrast, major stakeholders in the Danish plastic industry oppose the use of biode-
gradable plastics for general packaging purposes in general and food packaging. Their concerns 
center around the contamination of conventional plastic recycling streams and the lack of ade-
quate waste management infrastructure for biodegradable materials (Hann, Scholes, Lee, et al., 
2020). Instead, they advocate for using recycled conventional plastics or their bio-based versions 
e.g., polyethylene or polypropylene (see Figure 3-1 - Overview Bioplastics, for more detailed ex-
planation). 
 
Lastly, household composting is not considered a viable option, as biodegradable plastics do not 
adequately decompose under typical Danish home composting conditions (Werther et al., 2023). 
Furthermore, littering is not regarded as a relevant factor in Denmark (Bisinella et al., 2018). 
 
4.2.2 Raw Material Extraction, Preprocessing and VLPCB Manufacturing 
 
The study models the LCI for raw material extraction, preprocessing for further use, and VLPCB 
manufacturing in a cradle-to-gate approach. This decision follows the methodology applied in 
previous LCA studies (e.g., (Askham et al., 2021; Bisinella et al., 2018)) and makes use of avail-
able processes of the ecoinvent database. In line with this approach, the respective VLPCB man-
ufacturing process is modelled based on the ecoinvent data as a combination of two flows. For 
the respective material option, one of the following flows is used: (a) “polyethylene, low density, 
granulate” (for LDPE), (b) “polylactic acid, granulate” (for PLA) and (c) “polyester-complexed 
starch biopolymer” (for TPS1) and combined with the flow “extrusion, plastic film”. 
 
Together, the combination of raw material and extrusion process covers the full transformation 
from raw material extraction to extruded plastic films. Both input flows include all relevant process 
steps as well as product, elementary and waste flows necessary to result in either “polyethylene, 
low density, granulate” or “extrusion, plastic film”. This is due to its providers. A provider can be 
seen as a process that creates a product or in this case flows and models the upstream supply 
==PAGE:47==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
37 
chain to create a flow (GreenDelta Team, 2020). As an example, the flow “polyethylene, low den-
sity, granulate” includes, according to its global market provider, all activities and flows necessary 
to create “polyethylene, low density granulate” and ends with the transport to the consumer of the 
product (ecoinvent, 2025e). In this study, the consumer is the VLPCB manufacturing site.19  
 
The flow “extrusion, plastic film” models the conversion of thermoplastic material into to a plastic 
film. However, the provider description explicitly states that the thermoplastic material itself is not 
included in the upstream supply chain and must be included separately. Furthermore, 1kg of this 
converting process corresponds to the output of 0.976kg plastic film (ecoinvent, 2025c). 
 
To better reflect regional supply structures, customized manufacturing processes for all material 
types were created based on the former mentioned ecoinvent flows and process structure. This 
enables country specific modelling of the production phase using adjusted market compositions. 
Table 4-4 (see below) illustrates an example of the respective in- and outputs used for the mod-
elling of LDPE-based VLPCB manufacturing in Denmark. Further details for each country and 
material can be found in Appendix A-8.  
 
However, within the ecoinvent database, inventory data is only available for TPS1. TPS1 repre-
sents Mater-Bi, the commercial starch based raw material used by BioBag (see Section 4.2.1.2). 
As previously discussed, Mater-Bi is available in different variations with the biobased content 
ranging from 25% to 100% (Hann, Scholes, Lee, et al., 2020). The ecoinvent dataset used in this 
study corresponds to a 35% biobased composition (ecoinvent, 2025f). In order to assess the 
environmental performance of a fully biobased formulation, this study additionally models a hy-
pothetical TPS2 material composed of 100% biobased content. The modelling of TPS2 is based 
on consolidated data and assumptions derived from previous studies that report either LCI data 
or information regarding biobased TPS composition for plastic films. As detailed manufacturing 
data for 100% biobased Materi-Bi is neither publicly nor in ecoinvent available, an average of the 
relevant input flows was used to construct the inventory for the hypothetical model (for details see 
Appendix A-9). 
 
Furthermore, for modelling the LCI for the early life cycle stages, the following assumptions 
were made: 
 
(1) Exporting countries of LDPE-based VLPCBs: As presented in Section 4.2.1.2, plastic 
bags and sacks (incl. cones) made of polyethylene will serve as a proxy for VLPCB pro-
duction. These products are mainly imported from Europe and Asia with the biggest pro-
viders being Germany and Vietnam. Denmark has only a little domestic production share. 
To simplify the model, the study assumes that the whole production of VLPCBs made of 
LDPE in Europe are produced in Germany, from Asia in Vietnam as these are the biggest 
exporters of their continent. However, as regional markets are used in this model, the 
influence of this assumption is expected to be minimal. 
 
(2) Exporting countries for VLPCBs made of green materials: According to latest findings, 
(see Section 4.2.1.2) BioBag and Plant2Plast are the biggest exporting companies for 
 
19 Therefore, a separate modelling for transportating raw material to the VLPCB manufacturing site is not necessary 
(see Section 4.2.3). 
==PAGE:48==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
38 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
bags made of green materials to Denmark. The companies produce their products in 
Estonia, respectively China. To simplify the model, this study assumes that VLPCBs of 
the green materials PLA, TPS1, and TPS2 are mainly produced and imported from China 
and Estonia analogous to LDPE VLPCB modeling. Due to a lack of official statistics for 
the domestic production of Denmark, the same domestic production share used for LDPE 
VLPCB bags was applied in this context. 
 
(3) Supply chain analysis: ecoinvent offers data for a global market provider of “polyethylene, 
low density, granulate”. This provider includes a default product mix based on regional 
European (RER) and Rest-of-World (RoW) production shares. To better reflect the Dan-
ish, Chinese, Estonian, German and Vietnamese supply chains, this market mix was 
manually adapted based on the respective import data for low density polyethylene in 
primary forms (see Appendix A-10). For PLA, no differentiated regional markets as pro-
viders are available in ecoinvent v3.11 (APOS). Thus, the global provider was used with-
out modification. For TPS1, regional providers exist (RER and RoW). While country-spe-
cific trade statistics were not available, it was assumed that Estonian production sources 
TPS1 exclusively from RER and Chinese production exclusively from RoW market. This 
approach mirrors the import statistics for LDPE. Since TPS2 is a hypothetical case, no 
corresponding ecoinvent flow exists. Similar to TPS1, it was assumed that necessary 
material inputs required for TPS2 are sourced either entirely from RER or RoW markets. 
 
(4) Similar to assumption (3), ecoinvent offers multiple regional market providers for the flow 
“extrusion, plastic film” including: a global, RER, RoW, and a Canadian market. The study 
selected for each VLPCB manufacturing process the corresponding provider that best 
represents the regional production context. Specifically, RER was used for Denmark, Es-
tonia, and Germany, while RoW was used for Vietnam and China. This approach ensures 
that regional conditions are reflected as good as possible in the LCI model. 
 
(5) As ecoinvent v3.11 (APOS) only offers datasets for virgin material, the study assumes 
that all VLPCBs were made from them.

4.2.3 Transport and Distribution of VLPCBs 
 
Following the defined system boundaries, all relevant transport activities are modeled in as much 
detail as possible based on available data. To avoid double-counting, this study models transport 
processes only if they are not already accounted for in the respective unit processes or market 
activities provided by the ecoinvent database. For instance, the transport of raw materials (e.g., 
LDPE granulates) to the respective consumer (i.e., VLPCB manufacturing site) is already included 
in the chosen market activity for LDPE granulates. This logic applies to all materials. Therefore, 
no additional transport modeling is conducted for raw material extraction, preprocessing and sup-
ply to manufacturing facilities.  
 
In contrast, the following transport processes are modelled due to their relevance for the overall 
product system and their absence in the ecoinvent dataset: (a) transport of the finished VLPCBs 
from the manufacturing sites to Denmark, (b) national distribution to regional logistic hubs, (c) 
waste collection from households to treatment facilities. These transport activities are modeled 
based on geographically representative company locations, realistic routing assumptions, and 
consumption data of Danish regions. Detailed assumptions are explained in the following subsec-
tions. 
 
Transport activities related to the use phase (i.e., from retail to households) are excluded as the 
use phase is outside the defined system boundaries (see Section 4.1.2.3). 
 
4.2.3.1 Definition of representative VLPCB Manufacturing Companies 
 
To enable geographically specific modeling of international transport, representative VLPCB man-
ufacturers were selected for each country of origin. The selection of the manufacturing companies 
of domestic, German and Vietnamese production was based on the following criteria: (1) the 
company must be a major player in the food packaging market, (2) the company must offer fresh 
produce bags or plastic films for fresh produce, thus indicating the technical ability to manufacture 
VLPCBs, and (3) the company must offer products based on at least one of the materials consid-
ered in this study. For the production of VLPCBs in China and Estonia, the manufacturing com-
panies are already known (see Section 4.2.1.2). 
 
For Denmark, the subsidiary of Amcor Plc, Amcor Flexible Denmark ApS, was selected as a 
representative producer. Amcor Plc is a global leader in food packaging solutions and operates 
globally (Markets and Markets, 2025). Furthermore, according to market research, Amcor is 
amongst the largest manufacturers of flexible packaging in both Europe (Mordor Intelligence, 
2025a), and Denmark (Dun & Bradstreet, 2025).  
 
The following assumptions were made for the modeling of the domestic VLPCB production: 
 
(1) Product information provided by Amcor Flexible Denmark ApS shows that the company 
offers both flexible polyethylene-based bags (Amcor Plc, 2025b) as well as a PLA-based 
version for fresh produce (Amcor Plc, 2025a). Based on this information, this study as-
sumes that Amcor Flexibles Denmark ApS can produce VLPCBs made of all materials 
relevant to this study.  
==PAGE:53==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
43 
(2) All domestically produced VLPCBs are assumed to be produced by Amcor Flexible Den-
mark ApS. 
 
Likewise, Amcor Flexibles Teningen GmbH, a German subsidiary of Amcor Plc, was selected to 
represent the production in Germany. Given that both companies are subsidiaries, the following 
assumptions were made: 
 
(1) Given that Amcor Flexibles Denmark ApS and Amcor Flexibles Teningen GmbH are both 
subsidiaries of Amcor Plc, it is assumed that both manufacturing sites have the same 
technical capabilities. Consequently, the German subsidiary is capable of producing 
VLPCBs made of LDPE.  
(2) The German manufacturing site represents all VLPCBs produced in Germany, and by 
extension, all European LDPE-based VLPCB production. 
 
Vietnam represents the Asian production of LDPE-based VLPCBs. In this context, VN Plast CO., 
LTD. was selected as a representative company, as it also meets the predefined selection criteria. 
According to market research, VN Plast CO., LTD is one of the largest food packaging manufac-
turers in Vietnam and offers LDPE-based food bags for fresh produce (VN Plast CO., LTD, 
2024b). 
 
Based on this information, the following assumptions were made: 
 
(1) Product information provided by VN Plast CO. LTD shows that the company offers LDPE-
based food bags. Based on this information, this study assumes that VN Plast CO. can 
produce VLPCBs made of LDPE.  
(2) The manufacturing site represents all LDPE-based VLPCBs produced in Vietnam and, 
by extension, all Asian LDPE-based VLPCB. 
 
4.2.3.2 International Transport of VLPCBs to Denmark 
 
Based on the identified VLPCB manufacturing companies, international transport was modeled. 
Figure 4-6 shows the different transport routes as well as transport modes used from the respec-
tive manufacturing sites to Denmark. For the modeling of the international transport, the following 
assumptions were made: 
 
(1) From Germany to Denmark: 
• 
Transport is entirely conducted via road freight, following the assumptions of Bisi-
nella et al. (2018). 
• 
Transport is modelled up to the Danish border town of Padborg, as this town is one 
of Denmark’s largest logistic hubs, especially along the German border (Gate Den-
mark, International Transport Centre, 2025; Sørensen et al., 2008). 
• 
It is assumed that the shipment of VLPCBs manufactured in Germany are delivered 
in bulk to Padborg. Then redistribution to regional hubs takes place.  
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:54==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
44 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
(2) From Vietnam to Denmark: 
• 
The transport consists of three segments:  
(a) truck transport from the production facility to the nearest port (Hai Phong 
Port) (VN Plast CO., LTD, 2024a) 
(b) ocean freight from Hai Phong Port to Hamburg, as according to Fluent 
Cargo no direct maritime routes connect Hai Phong Port with the port of 
Aarhus (see Appendix A-11). 
(c) road transport from Hamburg to Padborg. 
 
(3) From Estonia to Denmark: 
• 
The production site is located on Hiiuuma (see Section 4.2.1.2). The island does not 
have a container terminal and is only served by domestic ferrys and vessels (Hiiu-
uma, 2025). Furthermore, there is no direct maritime route that connects Hiiuuma 
with Denmark (Hiumaa Arenduskeskus, 2025). Based on this information, it is as-
sumed that the transport consists of the following segments: 
(a) Road transportation from the VLPCB manufacturing site to the port of Hel-
termaa. 
(b) Ocean freight from the port of Heltermaa to the port of Rohuküla, which is 
located on the Estonian mainland. 
(c) Road transportation from the port of Rohuküla, to the port of Tallin. 
(d) Ocean freight from the port of Tallin to the port of Aarhus.  
 
(4) From China to Denmark: 
• 
Plant2Plast operates in different facilities in the provinces of Hunan, Shandong and 
Zhejiang. Shandong was selected as the province of the manufacturing site as the 
port of Qingdao offers a direct maritime route to Aarhus (see Appendix A-11). As no 
information was available on the location of the Plant2Plast manufacturing site in 
Shandong, the same road distance from manufacturing site to port as for Vietnam 
was assumed (see Appendix A-11). 
• 
The transport consists of the following segments. 
(a) Road transportation from the VLPCB manufacturing site to the Qingdao 
Port. 
(b) Ocean freight to the port of Aarhus. 
 
(5) All transport processes were modeled using the ecoinvent datasets as outlined in Table 
4-5. The transport routes and distances were based on publicly available data derived 
from Google Maps and Fluent Cargo20. Appendix A-11 provides detailed transport dis-
tances.

4.2.3.3 Distribution of VLPCBs within Denmark 
 
Once the VLPCBs are produced domestically or have arrived in Denmark from the exporting 
countries (China, Estonia, Germany, and Vietnam), they must be distributed nationwide. The 
study assumes that VLPCBs are used nationwide and are first delivered to regional logistic hubs 
before being forwarded to supermarkets or other retailers. To enable realistic modeling, the fol-
lowing assumptions were made: 
 
(1) Mode of transportation: VLPCBs are distributed within Denmark by road transport follow-
ing the assumptions made by Bisinella et al. (2018). The modeling of road transport was 
conducted analogously to the flow and provider combination described in Table 4-5. The 
ecoinvent flow “transport, freight, lorry, 16-32 metric ton, diesel, EURO 6” and the corre-
sponding market provider “market for transport, freight, lorry, 16-32 metric ton, diesel, 
EURO 6 | transport, freight, lorry, 16-32 metric ton, diesel, EURO 6 | APOS, U – RER” 
were used. 
 
(2) Mass allocation per region: Since this flow and provider combination represents the 
transport service of 1 metric ton per kilometer, the modeling accounts for the transport 
volume of each region. The transport volume was scaled based on the regional per capita 
consumption of VLPCBs (see Appendix A-12). This ensures that regional variations in 
transport demand and their resulting environmental impacts are reflected in the modeling.  
 
(3) Definition of regional logistic hubs: To model the regional distribution, representative lo-
cations for logistic hubs were assigned to the five Danish regions: Hovedstaden, Sjæl-
land, Midtjylland, Syddanmark, and Nordjylland. For each region, the official address of 
the respective regional administrative office was used as a proxy for the respective re-
gional hub location21.  
 
(4) Starting points of distribution: The transport starts either from the domestic VLPCB man-
ufacturing site (Amcor Flexibles Denmark ApS), the port of Aarhus (for imports from China 
and Estonia), or from Padborg (for imports from Germany and Vietnam).  
 
(5) Routing Assumptions: The transport distances were determined using publicly available 
data from Google Maps, applying the shortest available road route without ferry trans-
ports. This assumption is based on the expectation that all distribution occurs via road-
freight (see assumption (1)). 
 
Figure 4-7 illustrates the modeled national distribution network, showing all starting points, logistic 
hubs, and transport routes across the Danish regions. Exact transport distances are provided in 
Appendix A-11.

4.2.3.4 Waste Collection Transport 
 
The collection of the VLPCB waste from Danish households was modeled using the ecoinvent 
flow “municipal waste collection service by 21 metric ton lorry” in combination with its provider 
“municipal waste collection service by 21 metric ton lorry | municipal waste collection service by 
21 metric ton lorry | APOS, U – RoW”. As the providers are only available for Switzerland (CH) 
and Rest of World (RoW), the RoW provider was selected as, assuming the CH provider does 
not reflect Danish conditions.  
 
Region-specific data from Statbank Denmark was used to approximate realistic collection dis-
tances. The following parameters were considered: (a) land area covered by buildings in km2, (b) 
population data and (c) number of households for each Danish region. By using these input vari-
ables, the average distance between households in each Danish region was calculated (see Ap-
pendix A-14).  
 
The total collection distance for each Danish region was modeled based on the following assump-
tions: 
 
(1) Collection scope: Each household is assumed to be visited once for the VLPCB waste 
collection. Even though VLPCB waste is generated throughout the year, modeling multi-
ple collection rounds was avoided due to uncertainty in actual collection frequencies. 
Moreover, the simplified route modeling approach likely overestimates distances, which 
compensates for the assumption of a single annual collection round. 
 
(2) Distance Calculation: The total waste collection distance is calculated for each region as 
follows: 
 
Eq. (6) 
𝑤𝑎𝑠𝑡𝑒 𝑐𝑜𝑙𝑙𝑒𝑐𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛 𝑑𝑖𝑠𝑡𝑎𝑛𝑐𝑒  
𝑝𝑒𝑟 𝑟𝑒𝑔𝑖𝑜𝑛 
= (𝑎𝑣𝑒𝑟𝑎𝑔𝑒 𝑑𝑖𝑠𝑡𝑎𝑛𝑐𝑒 𝑏𝑒𝑡𝑤𝑒𝑒𝑛 ℎ𝑜𝑢𝑠𝑒ℎ𝑜𝑙𝑑𝑠 
×  𝑛𝑢𝑚𝑏𝑒𝑟 𝑜𝑓 ℎ𝑜𝑢𝑠𝑒ℎ𝑜𝑙𝑑𝑠)
+ (2 ×  𝑎𝑣𝑒𝑟𝑎𝑔𝑒 𝑑𝑖𝑠𝑡𝑎𝑛𝑐𝑒 𝑏𝑒𝑡𝑤𝑒𝑒𝑛 ℎ𝑜𝑢𝑠𝑒ℎ𝑜𝑙𝑑𝑠)
×  𝑟𝑜𝑢𝑡𝑖𝑛𝑔 𝑜𝑝𝑡𝑖𝑚𝑖𝑧𝑎𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛 𝑓𝑎𝑐𝑡𝑜𝑟 
 
The factor “average distance between households” multiplied by the number of house-
holds equals the total distance the lorry must travel in order to collect all VLPCB waste. 
2 times the “average distance between households” is added to account for an outbound 
trip from the waste management facility to the first household and one return trip from the 
last household back to the facility. Furthermore, a route optimization factor is added.  
 
(3) Route Optimization: Since assumption (2) simplifies the distance calculation for the mod-
eling purpose and does not account for, e.g., collection for multi-family buildings or 
smaller distances for denser urban infrastructure, the study applied a route optimization 
factor to reflect a more realistic routing. Based on route optimization studies for waste 
collection in the Danish context by Burkhal et al. (2012) and Zhib et al. (2019), a reduction 
factor of 0.6360 was used. This optimization factor represents an average based on the 
findings reported by Burkhal et al. (2012) and Zhib et al. (2019) (see Appendix A-14). 
 
==PAGE:59==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
49 
(4) Exclusion of transport to final treatment facilities: Subsequent transport of collected 
VLPCB waste to treatment facilities such as incineration, recycling, or industrial compost-
ing is not modelled separately. First, this due to the lack of realistic distance estimation, 
and second, it is assumed that the potentially overestimated collection distances suffi-
ciently capture additional transport to those facilities.  
 
(5) Waste Export: A portion of LDPE-based VLPCB waste is recycled abroad ((Eurostat, 
2025), see Section 4.2.4), particularly in Germany and the Netherlands (see Appendix A-
15). Without exact routing data, an average distance of 2000km is assumed for this 
transport, following the assumptions of (Bisinella et al., 2018). 
 
(6) Weight Assumption for Modelling: The selected flow requires distance and transported 
mass. Since the waste accumulates during collection, it would be incorrect to model the 
total lorry waste load over the entire distance. To approximate a more realistic load dis-
tribution, 50% of the transported waste for each region is set as the transported mass. 
This simplification approximates the average load carried by the truck throughout its col-
lection route, assuming that the VLPCB waste accumulates proportionally during collec-
tion. The total amount of VLPCB waste per region is based on a 100% waste collection 
rate (see Section 4.4.1.3). 
 
4.2.4 End-of-Life of VLPCBs 
 
The end-of-life treatment of VLPCBs is modeled in accordance with the current waste manage-
ment practices relevant to each material type. 
 
As outlined in Section 4.2.1.3, end-of-life treatment options differ depending on the material. For 
LDPE-based VLPCB waste, the following assumptions were made: 
 
(1) End-of-life options: The relevant end-of-life treatment options are incineration with energy 
recovery and recycling. 
 
(2) Waste Sorting: The VLPCBs are sorted prior to end-of-life treatment. Although sorting 
primarily serves to remove unwanted materials (e.g., metals, glass, paper, etc.), followed 
by preprocessing steps like washing to eliminate other contaminants like food residues 
or dirt (Lange, 2021), this study assumes a homogenous post-consumer LDPE-based 
VLPCB waste stream. Therefore, sorting is modeled as dividing the material into fractions 
for recycling and incineration. 
 
(3) Waste Mass Distribution: The distribution of VLPCB waste between the two treatment 
options is based on official waste statistics and additional assumptions: 
 
According to (Eurostat, 2025) the treatment of plastic packaging waste in Denmark in 
2022 was as follows (see also Appendix A-7): 
o 
76.5% incineration with energy recovery 
o 
13.5% recycling within Denmark 
o 
10.0% recycling outside of Denmark within EU (primarily in Germany and Den-
mark, see Appendix A-15) 
==PAGE:60==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
50 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
However, these figures refer to the outcome of the respective sorting steps (Eurostat, 
2025). As described in Section 4.2.1.3, the study assumes that approximately 9% of plas-
tic packaging waste does not enter the sorting chain and ends up in residual household 
waste for incineration. This share is transferred to the LDPE-based VLPCB end-of-life 
modeling. These 9% seem reasonable as of 2022 93 out of 98 local municipalities 
(94.9%) implemented the waste sorting scheme for plastics (Danish Environmental Pro-
tection Agency, n.d.). It is therefore assumed that 5% of municipalities lacked the waste 
sorting system, and an additional 4% of plastic waste in covered areas is still disposed of 
in residual waste.  
 
(4) Moreover, a recycling efficiency of 90% is assumed based on estimates by Lange (2021). 
This implies that 10% of the sorted recycling fraction cannot be effectively recycled and 
is therefore redirected to incineration.  
 
The resulting material flows for the end-of-life modeling of LDPE-based VLPCB waste are illus-
trated in Figure 4-8. 
 
 
Figure 4-8 - LDPE-based VLPCB Waste Flows 
Source: own illustration 
 
The relevant waste treatment options for VLPCBs made of PLA, TPS1, or TPS2 differ from those 
for LDPE. As described in Section 4.2.1.3, the end-of-life options are incineration with energy 
recovery and industrial composting. 
 
The following assumptions were made for modeling the end-of-life of VLCPBs made of green 
materials: 
 
(1) EoL options: All VLPCBs made of PLA, TPS1, or TPS2 are treated through incineration 
with energy recovery or industrial composting within Denmark. 
 
(2) Waste Sorting: Similar to the assumption for LDPE-based VLPCBs, this study assumes 
a homogenous post-consumer VLPCB waste stream for each material type. Conse-
quently, sorting is modeled as a mechanism to divide the material into fractions for incin-
eration and industrial composting. 
==PAGE:61==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
51 
(3) Waste Mass Distribution: Since no official statistics are available for the distribution of 
VLPCB waste between the two treatment options, this study adopts an estimation pro-
vided by Werther et al. (2023). They estimated that approximately 722 tonnes of biode-
gradable plastic food waste bags were used in 2023, of which 400 tonnes were directed 
to industrial composting. This corresponds to a composting share of 55.40%, which is 
assumed for all material types.  
 
Lastly, littering was excluded for all materials for two main reasons. First, data on plastic packag-
ing indicate that 100% of the plastic packaging placed on the market was recovered ((Eurostat, 
2025)(Appendix A-7)). Second, the packaging statistics report of the Danish Environmental Pro-
tection Agency highlights that insufficient data is available on littered waste, regardless of pack-
aging type (Musaeus & Djurhuus, 2024).

the LCI modeling is listed in Supplementary Information S1.

4.3 Planetary Boundary – Life Cycle Impact Assessment 
 
4.3.1 PB-LCIA Methodology, Impact Categories & Definition of SOS 
 
The Life Cycle Impact Assessment (LCIA) in this study is based on the PB-LCIA methodology 
developed by Ryberg, Owsianiak, Richardson, et al. (2018). The impact assessment method pro-
vides characterization factors (CF) that enable the direct expression of the environmental impacts 
in the PB framework metrics (Bjørn, Chandrakumar, et al., 2020; Clausen et al., 2024; Paulillo & 
Sanyé-Mengual, 2024; Sala et al., 2020), allowing a direct comparison with the PB control varia-
bles (see Section 3.7.1.). The impact assessment method is chosen in line with the study goal. 
 
The PB-LCIA method includes 85 CFs covering seven out of the nine PB processes. The method 
excludes the PB processes “Biosphere Integrity” and “Novel Entities” due to methodological limi-
tations at the time of method development. More specifically, for “Biosphere Integrity” characteri-
zation models for delineating CFs were considered immature, and for “Novel Entities”, the PB 
was not yet defined (Ryberg, Owsianiak, Richardson, et al., 2018). As such, these PB processes 
are excluded from this study.  
 
It should be noted that specific PB processes are represented by multiple impact categories within 
the PB-LCIA method. This occurs for two main reasons: (1) First, specific PB processes are de-
fined by multiple control variables. For instance, the PB process “Climate Change” is represented 
by radioactive forcing and CO2 concentration (see Section 3.5 and Richardson et al. (2023). 
Therefore, two impact categories have been defined by Ryberg et al. (2018): “Energy imbalance 
at top-of-atmosphere (for radioactive forcing)” and “Atmospheric CO2 concentration”. (2) Second, 
for some PB processes, spatial differentiation is necessary, as characterized LCI results cannot 
be meaningfully aggregated into a single global score. Thus, spatially differentiated impact cate-
gories were established for specific PB processes, e.g., Land System Change or Freshwater 
Change. In order to provide a comprehensive AESA study, all impact categories for which CFs 
are available and corresponding elementary flows exist in ecoinvent v3.11 (APOS), were included 
in this study. Table 4-7 (below) presents an overview of the considered impact categories for this 
study, their corresponding PB process as well as the defined Safe Operating Space (SOS). The 
definition of the SOS is explained in the paragraph below the table.  
 
Excluded Impact Categories 
The following impact categories were excluded for the reasons outlined below: 
 
(1) Land System Change (regional): In line with the findings of Nørrekær Lund et al. (2023), 
the current ecoinvent v3.11 (APOS) database lacks spatial resolution of land-change 
flows to distinguish between the different biome types mentioned by Ryberg et al. (2018). 
Consequently, only the global land system change impact category is considered in this 
study. 
(2) Phosphorus Flow (regional) & Atmospheric Aerosol Loading (regional): Due to the glob-
alized supply chains for the VLPCBs included in this LCA and the complexity of allocating 
regional flows across the involved countries, only the global impact categories of Phos-
phorus Flow and Atmospheric Aerosol Loading were assessed. Accounting for all re-
gional variations would exceed the feasible, timely scope of this study. 
==PAGE:64==

Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
As described in Section 3.5, the PBs are set limits in which humanity can operate safely without 
destabilizing the ES (Richardson et al., 2023; Rockström et al., 2009; Steffen et al., 2015). How-
ever, for specific PB processes, nature already occupies a portion of the respective SOS. This 
natural occupation is referred to as natural background level (see Section 3.6.2.1) (Hjalsted et al., 
2021; Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual, 2024; Ryberg, Owsianiak, Clavreul, et al., 2018). The preindus-
trial Holocene base value represents the portion of the respective natural background level 
(Hjalsted et al., 2021) and must be considered when defining the SOS for human activities. For 
most PB processes, subtracting the portion occupied by nature from the PB value defines the 
available SOS for humanity.  
 
However, for the PB processes “Land System Change”, “Stratospheric Ozone Depletion”, and 
“Ocean Acidification”, this relationship is inverted. For these PB processes, a decrease from the 
preindustrial baseline indicates the degradation of the ES state. The PB value thus defines a 
minimum threshold that must not be crossed. Consequently, the SOS corresponds to the differ-
ence between the preindustrial state and this minimum threshold. For instance, the PB for the PB 
process “Land System Change” is defined as 75% of the remaining forest area relative to the 
preindustrial baseline of 100%. Therefore, the SOS for anthropogenic land-use change is a max-
imum of 25%. 
 
4.3.2 PB-LCIA Dataset Update 
 
For the LCIA, an adapted version of the dataset PB-LCIA2.03_OpenLCA (Nørrekær Lund et al., 
2023) was applied. The dataset was initially developed by Nørrekær Lund, Schjerbeck, and 
Winther Schor (Nørrekær Lund et al., 2023) and is based on the original PB-LCIA impact assess-
ment dataset developed for SimaPro by Ryberg et al. (2020). Nørrekær Lund, Schjerbeck, and 
Winther Schor initially converted the SimaPro dataset to be usable in OpenLCA 1.11 and 
OpenLCA 2.0 beta (Nørrekær Lund et al., 2023). However, a comprehensive review of their con-
verted PB-LCIA dataset revealed that substantial modifications were necessary to ensure com-
patibility with the versions of OpenLCA and the ecoinvent database used for this study. 
 
To ensure methodological consistency and data completeness, a systematic comparison be-
tween the following data sources was conducted: (1) the converted PB-LCIA2.03_OpenLCA 
(Nørrekær Lund et al., 2023) – herein after referred to as the converted PB-LCIA dataset. (2) The 
methodology paper: “Development of a life-cycle impact assessment methodology linked to the 
Planetary Boundaries framework” (Ryberg, Owsianiak, Richardson, et al., 2018), which outlines 
the underlying characterization factor calculations and provides a table of the 85 characterization 
factors (CFs) grouped by impact category. (3) The case study paper: “Absolute environmental 
sustainability assessment of a Danish utility company relative to the Planetary Boundaries” 
(Ryberg, Bjerre, et al., 2020). This paper, as well as its supplementary information, including life 
cycle inventory data and CFs used within the study, served as the basis for the dataset conversion 
conducted by Nørrekær Lund, Schjerbeck, and Winther Schor (2023). 
 
 
 
==PAGE:67==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
57 
The validation and update processes were performed in two main steps: 
 
Step 1: Verification of characterization factors 
For each impact category, the CFs implemented in the converted dataset were compared against 
the original values from the methodology paper and the case study paper. This step was neces-
sary, as in the case study paper Ryberg et al. (2020) adapted specific elementary flows and CFs 
to better fit their study context. These adapted flows and CFs were subsequently included in the 
converted PB-LCIA dataset. As a result, the anticipated discrepancies between the converted PB-
LCIA dataset and the methods paper were identified. All deviations were reviewed (see Appendix 
A-4) and the CFs corrected (Step 2) where necessary to align with the original PB-LCIA method-
ology.  
 
Step 2: Cross-check and correction of elementary flows 
All elementary flows included in the converted PB-LCIA dataset were reviewed and compared 
with the standard elementary flows available in ecoinvent v3.11 (APOS). As highlighted by 
Norrekær Lund et al. (2023), structural differences between LCA software can lead to mis-
matches. For instance, SimaPro organizes flows under “compartments” and “subcompartments”, 
whereas OpenLCA uses “categories” and “subcategories”. However, discrepancies are not lim-
ited to structural differences among software systems alone. Also, the interoperability between 
different LCI and LCIA data sources poses a well-known challenge. Differences in flow nomen-
clature across databases can impede the correct linking of CFs to LCI flows, which in turn may 
affect the results of the assessment (Valente et al., 2024). Therefore, cross-checking was neces-
sary for two reasons: a) adapted elementary flows by Ryberg et al. (2020) and interoperability 
challenges of different systems and data sources (Valente et al., 2024), 
 
As anticipated, specific elementary flows were identified that did not match the standard ecoinvent 
v3.11 (APOS) naming convention. For instance, the converted PB-LCIA dataset included the flow 
“Ethane, 1,1,2-trichloro-1,2,2-trifluoro-,CFC-113” in the respective impact categories, whereas the 
corresponding ecoinvent flow is titled “1,1,2-trichloro-1,2,2-trifluoroethane”. During the import of 
the converted PB-LCIA database in OpenLCA, these unmatched flows were added as new entries 
in the flow hierarchy. Since these newly introduced flows are not part of the standard ecoinvent 
v3.11 (APOS) database, they are not linked to existing inventory processes during impact as-
sessment. 
 
All elementary flows of the converted PB-LCIA dataset were reviewed to address this issue. All 
non-matching flows were replaced by the corresponding default flows available in ecoinvent v3.11 
(APOS). This correction process followed a structured three-step approach:  
 
1. CAS Number Matching: Many flows in the converted PB-LCIA dataset included Chemical 
Abstract Service (CAS) numbers in their flow information. The CAS number is a unique 
numeric identification number for chemical compounds (American Chemical Society, 
2025), which enabled precise identification of the corresponding flow in ecoinvent v3.11 
(APOS). For flows without CAS numbers, the flow name was searched in PubChem to 
retrieve the correct CAS number for matching in ecoinvent. 
 
==PAGE:68==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
58 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
2. Validation via PubChem: All CAS numbers were verified using PubChem to confirm the 
chemical identity of the elementary flow and correct potential misclassifications due to 
incorrect CAS assignments. 
 
3. Integration of correct default flows in impact categories: The identified default flows from 
ecoinvent were added to the respective impact categories. The respective CFs defined 
by Ryberg, Owsianiak, Richardson, et al. (2018), and the respective regional information, 
defined by Ryberg et al. (2020)22, were then assigned to these flows, to enable regional-
ized assessments.  
 
In total, 1,052 elementary flows were updated to match the naming conventions and data struc-
ture of ecoinvent v3.11 (APOS) and OpenLCA v2.4.1. Depending on the impact category, the 
share of adapted flows ranges from 3.85% to 100%23. For a complete list of updated flows, see 
Supplementary Information S2.2. The corresponding JSON-file is added as Supplementary Infor-
mation S2.1.  
 
4.4 Assigning Share of Safe Operating Space 
 
To perform the AESA study, it is necessary to split and assign a share of the SOS the studied 
system (Guinée et al., 2022). As stated in Section 3.6.2, the two step-approach is used to derive 
this allocation to the VLPCBs. In the following the two alternatives discussed in Section 3.6.2 are 
presented in detail: (1) equal per capita & FCE principle, (2) ability to pay & FCE principle. Based 
on these principles, the assigned percentage SoSOS assigned to the studied system (aSPB,SP) is 
calculated. This percent wise share is then used in Section 4.6.2 to quantify the SoSOS to the 
VLPCBs.  
 
4.4.1 Defining the SoSOSEPC, FCE 
 
For calculating the aSPB,EPC&FCE, the study applies the two-step approach proposed by Ryberg et 
al. (2018), using the equal per capita (EPC) and final consumption expenditure (FCE) sharing 
principles. This approach multiplies (i) the ratio of the national to the global population and (ii) the 
ratio of the FCE for the studied product to the total FCE of the respective country (Denmark), 
yielding an aSPB,EC&FCE of 0.0000013% (see Eq. (7)).

in the supplementary information. Within this dataset, countries are linked to specific elementary flows and the respective

𝑉𝐿𝑃𝐶𝐵 
 
 
=  33,783,460.41 𝐷𝐾𝐾 
 
The price of 0.43 DKK per VLPCB was derived from publicly available Danish retail offers for 
LPCBs. As Danish retailers did not offer VLPCBs at the time of data collection, the average LPCB 
retail price was used as a proxy to estimate the market price of VLPCBs. The data basis for this 
estimation includes a range of LPCB offerings from different Danish retailers to account for size 
variations, and purchase quantity (see Appendix A-2). However, only LPCBs that fulfil the criteria 
of (a) being made of LDPE, (b) with a wall thickness ≤ 50 microns, and (c) food safety standards 
were included in the price calculation. These criteria were chosen to comply with the EU directive 
and to represent the status quo as best as possible. To enable a meaningful comparison with 
VLPCBs made of PLA and TPS, LPCB offers containing recycled or bio-based LDPE were delib-
erately excluded from the price assessment. This decision is based on two reasons: (a) virgin 
LDPE-based VLPCBs serve as the baseline scenario for this study (see Section 4.1.2.2), and (b) 
no publicly available data could be identified regarding either the actual share of recycled and bio-
based LDPE in the potential LPCB offers, which would have been necessary to justify any as-
sumptions regarding their quantified relevance for the defined FU. 
 
4.4.2 Defining the SoSOSATP, FCE 
 
The ATP principle stems from prioritarianism and prioritizes those in the most disadvantaged 
positions (Holtug & Lippert-Rasmussen, 2006). In the AESA context, the ATP principle allocates 
==PAGE:70==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
60 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
greater shares of SOS to countries with lower GDP per capita (Hjalsted et al., 2021). This ap-
proach ensures that less developed countries are not overburdened with environmental respon-
sibilities, while more developed nations must take greater responsibilities. Thereby, economically 
developed countries are held to higher standards regarding their environmental responsibility. 
Thus, the ATP principle assigns a smaller SoSOS specifically to countries that can financially 
afford to reduce pollution or enhance their environmental performance (Hjalsted et al., 2021; 
Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual, 2024). In this study, the calculation of the aSPB,ATP&FCE follows the 
methodology outlined by (Hjalsted et al., 2021), resulting in aSPB,ATP&FCE of 0.0000000572% (see 
Eq. (9)). 
 
Eq. (9)

by the GDP per capita of the studied nation with a scaling factor . The ladder ensures that the 
total SOS does not exceed its limit. For details of calculating , refer to Eq. (10) below.

4.4.3 Quantifying SoSOS based on the Allocation Principles 
 
To quantify the SoSOS based on the respective percentage allocation share aSPB,SP, the following 
formula is applied to all SOS for humanity values across the impact categories considered in this 
study: 
 
Eq. (11)

Here, SoSOSic, SP denotes the share of Safe Operating Space for impact category ic that is allocated 
to the VLPCBs under sharing principle SP. The variable aSPB, SP is the corresponding allocation 
factor calculated based on the allocation principles described in the preceding Sections. In this 
study, SoSOS1 is based on the EPC & FCE principle, while SoSOS2 is based on the ATP & FCE 
principle. The resulting quantified values for SoSOS1 and SoSOS2 are listed in Table 4-10 below.

5. Results and Discussion 
This chapter presents the results of the AESA of VLPCBs made from LDPE, PLA, and thermo-
plastic starch (TPS1 and TPS2). The environmental impacts were calculated for the defined FU 
(see Section 4.1.2.1), representing Denmark’s annual consumption of VLPCBs in 2022. The cal-
culation applied the PB-LCIA methodology defined by Ryberg, Owsianiak, Richardson, et al. 
(2018), using an updated version of the PB_LCIA2.03_OpenLCA dataset developed by Nørrekær 
Lund, Schjerbeck, and Winther Schor (2023). The updated PB-LCIA dataset version was adapted 
to OpenLCA v2.4.1 and ecoinvent v3.11 (APOS) (see Section 4.3.2) and is documented in Sup-
plementary Information S2. 
The following sections lay out and interpret the AESA results. Section 5.1 presents the core re-
sults for the FU of the AESA study based on the LCI modeling described in Section 4.4 and the 
two-step allocation approach using the Equal per capita and FCE principles defined in Section 
3.6.2.2 and 4.4. The characterized LCI results are compared against the assigned SoSOSEPC,FCE 
to the VLPCBs. For each impact category the ESR, defined as the ratio of environmental impact 
to the assigned assigned SoSOSEPC,FCE is calculated. The ESR metric serves as the key metric 
to evaluate whether the respective material option is considered absolutely sustainable. However, 
as discussed in Section 3.5, all PBs must be respected simultaneously to achieve continuous ES 
stability. 
The alternative allocation approach, using the Ability-to-Pay and FCE principles (presented in 
Section 4.4.2), will be explored as part of the Sensitivity Analysis in Section 5.2. This is done to 
assess the robustness of the results against different allocation assumptions following the Euro-
pean Commission’s AESA Guidelines (Bjørn et al., 2025) and recent AESA studies (e.g., Paulillo 
& Sanyé-Mengual (2024); Yang & Paulillo (2025). Furthermore, the sensitivity analysis explores 
how changes in key parameters and allocation assumptions affect the overall results. 
Afterwards, section 5.3 presents the Uncertainty Analysis based on a Monte Carlo Simulation to 
quantify the variability of the results due to uncertainty in the input data. Lastly, Section 5.4 dis-
cusses the results and compares the findings with existing literature. Furthermore, the section 
contains a reflection on study limitations and provides implications for future studies. 
 
5.1 AESA Results 
 
Figure 5-1 and Table 5-1 present the core AESA results. Figure 5-1 displays the ESR scores 
across the 12 impact categories presented in Section 4.3.1, whereas Table 5-1 lists the charac-
terized LCI results and the corresponding occupation of the assigned SoSOSEPC,FCE expressed in 
percent. An ESR score of ≤ 1 indicates that the environmental impact of the respective material 
remains within its assigned SoSOSEPC,FCE, and is therefore considered sustainable for the specific 
impact category. Conversely, an ESR value > 1 represents a transgression of the assigned 
SoSOSEPC,FCE and is therefore considered unsustainable for the respective impact category. How-
ever, as described in Section 3.7.3, the FU based on the different materials can only be classified 
as absolutely sustainable if all ESRs for one material remain ≤ 1 across all impact categories. 
==PAGE:74==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
64 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
This criterion ensures compliance with the strong sustainability interpretation of the PB frame-
work, which does not allow for trade-offs between boundaries to maintain a stable ES (see Section 
3.5). 
The results demonstrate that none of the four investigated material options can be considered 
absolutely sustainable, as all materials exceed the assigned SoSOSEPC,FCE in most impact cate-
gories. This finding implies that Denmark’s 2022 VLPCB consumption cannot be sustained within 
the planetary boundaries based on the given material options, model assumptions, and allocation 
approach. 
The most significant transgressions of the assigned SoSOSEPC,FCE are observed for the PB pro-
cess “Climate Change” within the impact categories “Atmospheric CO2 concentration” and “En-
ergy imbalance at top of atmosphere”. In both impact categories, all studied materials exceed 
their assigned SoSOSEPC,FCE by a large margin, as the occupation shares range from approx. 
6,800% to 7,900% for “atmospheric CO2 concentration”, and 6,300% to 7,500% for “energy im-
balance at top of atmosphere”. In contrast, the lowest occupation shares are found in the “Strat-
ospheric Ozone Depletion” impact category ranging from 0.17% to 0.21% for all materials.  
 
 
Figure 5-1 - Environmental Sustainability Ratio – SoSOSEPC,FCE 
Source: own illustration 
Among the four assessed materials, LDPE performed best in the AESA. Figure 5-1 shows that 
LDPE-based VLPCBs remained within their assigned SoSOSEPC,FCE in three out of twelve impact 
categories. Furthermore, LDPE exhibits the lowest environmental impact scores in nine out of 
twelve categories (see Table 5-1) and therefore shows the least transgression of the assigned 
SoSOSEPC,FCE compared to the green material alternatives. In the remaining three impact catego-
ries “Freshwater Change – Global”, “Freshwater Change – Humid”, and “Stratospheric Ozone 
Depletion”, TPS2 shows the lowest environmental impact scores. However, it is important to note 
that TPS2 represents a hypothetical case based on a 100% biobased TPS formulation, as de-
scribed in Section 4.1.1, and is therefore not commercially available.
