Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
When restricting the comparison against the commercially available materials, LDPE outperforms 
the green materials in all impact categories, consistently exhibiting the lowest impact scores. This 
finding suggests that LDPE is Denmark’s most favorable material option for VLPCBs under the 
applied modeling assumptions and material options.  
However, this outcome may largely be influenced by the fact that, due to a lower density, LDPE 
requires approximately 26% less material input than PLA, and about 28% less than TPS to fulfil 
the defined FU (ref. Reference Flow Section 4.1.2.2). This difference in material consumption 
propagates throughout the entire life cycle as it influences most of the modeled processes. 
Among the green materials, the hypothetical TPS2 performs best, as it has the lowest impact 
scores in ten out of twelve impact categories (see Table 5-1), and therefore showing the smallest 
exceedance of the SoSOSEPC,FCE within these categories. When comparing the two commercially 
available green materials, TPS1 (reference product Mater-Bi) and PLA (reference product pro-
duced by NatureWorks), TPS1 outperforms PLA. TPS1 shows lower impact scores in eleven out 
of twelve impact categories. The performance gap is particularly pronounced in the impact cate-
gories “Atmospheric Aerosol Loading”, “Biogeochemical Flows: Phosphorus”, “Biogeochemical 
Flows: Nitrogen”, “Freshwater Use Global”, and “Freshwater Use – Basin Dry”. In these catego-
ries, the difference in SoSOSEPC,FCE transgression between TPS1 and PLA exceeds 500%, with 
the most significant disparity observed in “Biogeochemical Flows: Nitrogen”, where PLA exceeds 
the SoSOSEPC,FCE by nearly 2000% more than TPS1 (see Table 5-1). 
These results indicate that although TPS1 is only partially biobased (approximately 35%, see 
Section 4.1.1), it performs better than the 100%-biobased PLA. This difference is likely explained 
by the lower demand for agricultural inputs, such as fertilizer during the “raw material extraction 
and preprocessing” stage of TPS1. 
Although both materials rely on maize as feedstock (ecoinvent, 2025f, 2025h), PLA requires 
higher quantities per functional unit, since TPS1 is blended with fossil components. This results 
in higher contributions to the impact categories “Biogeochemical Flows: P” and “Biogeochemical 
Flows: N”, where the differences between PLA and TPS1 are particularly pronounced. As shown 
in Figure 5-224, for PLA, 79.87% of total phosphorus-related emissions in this impact category are 
linked to the “Raw Material Extraction and Preprocessing Stage”, compared to only 34.99% for 
TPS1. Furthermore, 70% of these emissions for PLA are directly attributable to maize produc-
tion25, while the corresponding share for TPS1 is approximately 24% (see Supplementary Infor-
mation S3.1). A similar pattern is observed for nitrogen-related emissions: For PLA, 73.54% of 
the total nitrogen-related emissions stem from the “Raw Material Extraction and Preprocessing 
Stage”, of which 48% are directly linked to maize production. In contrast, TPS1 shows only 
39.04% from that stage, with just 12% directly attributable to maize (Supplementary Information 
S3.1). Similar patterns are observed in the “freshwater use”-related impact categories in Figure 
5-2. Further, the contribution analysis provides additional insights. It reveals which life cycle 
stages contribute most to impact categories (Goulart Coelho & Lange, 2018). For this purpose, 
the LCIA results were aggregated and visualized according to the life cycle stages defined in 
Section 4.1.2.3 and color-coded in Figure 5-2 as follows: Cradle-to-gate (green), Transport & 
Distribution (blue), End-of-life (red). 
 
24 Quantified values are listed in Supplementary Information S3.1. 
25 The used ecoinvent v3.11 (APOS) processes in the LCI refer to maize production. Maize production datasets include 
all inputs and process steps from cultivating maize to harvesting and ending at the farm gate (ecoinvent, 2025d). 
==PAGE:77==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
67 
 
Figure 5-2 - Impact of Life Cycle Stages on Impact Categories 
Source: own illustration - All percentage values for the individual life cycle stages were derived from the impact analysis and contribution tree tab of the calculated 
LCIA results. 
==PAGE:78==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
68 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
The contribution analysis shows that, under the given modeling assumptions, “Cradle-to-gate” 
and “Transport & Distribution” are the two dominant contributors across most impact categories. 
In five out of twelve impact categories, namely “Freshwater Use – Global”, “Freshwater Use – 
Basin Dry”, “Freshwater Use – Basin Semidry”, “Freshwater Use – Basin Humid” & “Land System 
Change”, the “cradle-to-gate” life cycle stage accounts for more than 60% of the total impact for 
all assessed materials. The most significant share is found in the impact category “Freshwater 
Use – Global”, where the “cradle-to-gate” life-cycle stage contributes between 74.12% (TPS2) 
and 89.05% (LDPE). 
While one might assume that the green materials dominate the “Freshwater Use – Global” impact 
category due to their agricultural origin, the highest contribution is actually observed for LDPE. 
This is mainly because freshwater is also used for energy generation. In fact, in the case of LDPE, 
approximately 58% of the total freshwater use across the LDPE-based FU life cycle is attributed 
to energy production, of which the majority (58%) is then used in the early life cycle stages. How-
ever, this observation is not limited to LDPE. Across all materials, more than 50% of the global 
freshwater use is linked to energy generation used for the early life cycle stages: approximately 
65% - PLA, 59% - TPS1, and 62% - TPS2. This finding showcases that most freshwater impacts 
in this study are related to energy-intensive processes in the “cradle-to-gate” life cycle stage, 
rather than crop cultivation alone (see Bachmann (2024) for similar results). 
The impact category “Land System Change - Global” showcases a different pattern. While the 
“Cradle-to-gate” life cycle stage still accounts for a considerable share of approximately 71% for 
LDPE, this impact category is almost exclusively driven by the “Raw Material Extraction and Pre-
processing” phase in the case of green materials, with shares of approximately 86% of impacts 
for TSP1 and 96% of impacts for PLA and TPS2. This corresponds to values more than 3.4 times 
higher than for LDPE, where the same stage contributes only 25%, whereas the remaining 46% 
are related to the VLPCB manufacturing. 
The reasons for these differences in global land system change are tied to the nature of the 
materials. In the LDPE-based FU life cycle, over one- third of the land system is attributable to (1) 
construction of onshore petroleum field infrastructure (17%) and (2) forestry for energy production 
(21%). For PLA, 94% of global land use change is agriculture-related, with 86% directly caused 
by maize production and 8% linked to land transformation from forest to cropland. TPS1 and 
TPS2 show similar patterns. For both materials 81% respectively 86% of global land use change 
is related to the cultivation of different crops (maize, soybean, rapeseed), and 6% respectively 
3% is related to deforestation for agriculture. These findings display the strong land-use intensity 
of green materials. Nonetheless, it must be noted, despite these high contributions, all materials 
remain within their assigned SoSOSEPC,FCE (see Figure 5-1 and Table 5-1).  
The following section now focuses on the environmental impacts associated with the “Transport 
& Distribution” stage, which emerged as a major contributor in the five impact categories: “Atmos-
pheric Aerosol Loading”, “Atmospheric CO2 concentration”, “Energy Imbalance at top-of-atmos-
phere”, “Stratospheric Ozone Depletion” & “Ocean Acidification”. In each impact category, the 
“VLPCB Waste Collection” phase is the dominant source of impacts, contributing at least 50% of 
the total environmental burden across all materials. On average, 67.39% of the impacts in these 
five categories are attributable to waste collection activities. The highest contributions from the 
VLPCB waste collection are found in the categories “Atmospheric CO2 concentration” and “Ocean 
Acidification”, where TPS1 and TPS2 show the highest values of 72.27% and 79.50%. On the 
one hand, this finding highlights that downstream life cycle stages, especially waste logistics, can 
==PAGE:79==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
69 
be equally decisive and should not be overlooked. On the other hand, it must be acknowledged 
that, as mentioned in Section 4.4.3.4, the VLPCB waste collection distance modeling is based on 
best possible approximations that may significantly overestimate real-world conditions. This may 
also explain the high impact shares attributed to the “VLPCB Waste collection” life cycle stage. 
In contrast to the previously discussed categories, which are dominated either by the “Cradle-to-
Gate” or “Transport & Distribution” life cycle stages, the “End-of-Life” life cycle stages play a minor 
role in most impact categories, based on the given assumptions and modeling. Only in the cate-
gories “Biogeochemical Flows: P” and “Biogeochemical Flows: N” do EoL stages contribute more 
substantially, particularly for green materials. This can be attributed to releasing nitrogen and 
phosphate compounds during composting and incineration of the biobased materials (Islam et al., 
2024). In contrast, LDPE incineration or recycling only releases nutrient emissions in significant 
amounts as the material lacks nitrogen or phosphorus content. However, across all other impact 
categories, the contribution of EoL life cycle stages remains marginal. Industrial composting ac-
counts on average for only 2.15%, whereas incineration contributes on average for 2.10%. 
For LPDE, EoL stages are only contributing in relevant amounts in three impact categories: At-
mospheric CO2 Concentration”, “Energy Imbalance at top-of-atmosphere” and “Ocean Acidifica-
tion”. In these categories, incineration is responsible for an average of 18.61% of the total envi-
ronmental impacts. In contrast, recycling contributes the least across all categories with a share 
of 2.26% in “Biogeochemical Flows: N”.  
 
5.2 Sensitivity Analysis 
 
The sensitivity analysis aims to evaluate the robustness of the AESA results under variations in 
key assumptions and model parameters (ISO, 2006, 2021). For this study, three complementary 
sensitivity approaches were applied: (1) Assessment of an alternative SoSOS allocation ap-
proach, (2) parametric one-at-a-time (OAT) sensitivity analysis, and (3) scenario analysis of com-
bined system changes. 
 
In line with current AESA guidance from the European Commission (Bjørn et al., 2025) and cur-
rent AESA studies (e.g., Ghani et al. (2023); Kyttä et al. (2025) and Masjedi et al., 2024), testing 
alternative approaches is essential, as the choice of allocation principles heavily influences the 
overall AESA result (see Section 3.6.2.2). Therefore, the alternative allocation approach based 
on the ATP and FCE principles (described in Section 4.4.2) is evaluated and discussed in Section 
5.2.1.  
 
In the second step, selected input parameters from the different life cycle stages were varied 
individually in the OAT analysis. Each parameter was altered by -10%. For each variation, a Sen-
sitivity Coefficient was calculated to quantify the parameter’s influence and to identify critical as-
sumptions. The results of this OAT analysis are presented in Section 5.2.2. 
 
Lastly, a scenario-based sensitivity analysis was conducted in which interdependent parameters 
or system-wide changes were varied to assess their influence on the AESA results. For instance, 
a higher recycling rate and consequently a lower incineration rate for LDPE-based VLPCBs was 
assessed to reflect a possible future scenario. The results of these combined system-level 
changes are presented in Section 5.2.3. 
==PAGE:80==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
70 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
5.2.1 Alternative SoSOS Allocation Approach 
 
Figure 5-3 presents the AESA results based on the alternative SoSOS allocation approach ap-
plying the ATP & FCE principles. As described in Section 4.4.2, this approach allocates the SoSO-
SATP,FCE more stringently to countries with higher financial capabilities. As anticipated, the stricter 
allocation leads to fewer impact categories being classified as sustainable.  
 
Since the underlying LCI data and modeling assumptions remain unchanged, only the relative 
performance against the lower SoSOSATP,FCE shifts. The newly applied SoSOSATP,FCE is approxi-
mately 1.36 orders of magnitude (equivalent to a 23-fold reduction) more stringent than 
SoSOSEPC,FCE. As a result, the ESR scores shift accordingly.  
 
The LDPE-based VLPCBs, which previously remained within their assigned SoSOSEPC,FCE in 
three categories, now comply with the SoSOSATP,FCE in only two. Similarly, the green materials 
formerly classified as sustainable in the two categories “Stratospheric Ozone Depletion” and 
“Land System Change - Global”, now meet the stricter threshold only in the former. 
 
This outcome highlights the significant normative influence of different allocation choices on 
AESA results. Stricter allocation approaches can shift systems from seemingly sustainable to 
unsustainable classification and vice versa, without changing the LCI and modeling assumptions. 
Given this, it underlies the importance of transparency when conducting an AESA study, espe-
cially in contexts where results inform policy or strategic decision-making (Bjørn et al., 2025).  
 
 
Figure 5-3 - Environmental Sustainability Ratio – SoSOSATP,FCE 
Source: own illustration

Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach

5.2.2 One-at-a-Time Analysis 
 
To evaluate the sensitivity of the AESA results to specific LCI modeling parameters, an OAT 
sensitivity analysis was conducted, as recommended by Heijungs (2021). In this approach, se-
lected parameters were perturbed individually by -10%, while all other parameters were held con-
stant. Although arbitrary values, e.g., ± 10% or 20% can be assessed (Mahmood et al., 2022), 
this study applied only a reduction of 10%. This decision is justified by the presented AESA results 
in Section 5.1, as in most of the impact categories, the assigned SoSOSEPC,FCE is already ex-
ceeded. Therefore, the different VLPCB material options studied are not considered absolutely 
sustainable. Increasing parameter values by +10% would likely only worsen the AESA outcomes 
and provide limited additional insights within the scope of this study. However, a +10% increase 
could test whether the perturbation transgresses the currently sustainable impact categories. Fu-
ture studies could explore this. 
 
Following the previous AESA study of Ryberg, Owsianiak, Clavreul, et al. (2018), the influence of 
each perturbed parameter on the impact scores (IS) was quantified using Sensitivity Coefficients 
(Scoef) (Eq. (12)) as described by (Heijungs, 2010): 
 
Eq. (12) 
Scoef 
= ∆IS
IS0
∆ak
ak,0
⁄
 
 
ak,0: 
Default model parameter 
 
IS0: 
Impact Score without perturbations  
 
∆ak: 
Delta between default and perturbed model parameter 
 
∆IS: 
Delta between IS0 and calculated IS after perturbation 
 
Depending on the Scoef value, a modeling parameter (ak,0) can have a varying importance. How-
ever, a modeling parameter can be considered sensitive with a value of Scoef ≥ 0.05 (Ryberg, 
Owsianiak, Clavreul, et al., 2018).  
 
Modeling parameters that were included in the OAT analysis are: (a) extrusion, plastic film (energy 
and auxiliary requirements for VLPCB manufacturing), (b) transport distance of VLPCBs to Den-
mark, (c) transport distance of VLPCBs within Denmark, (d) VLPCB waste collection distance, (e) 
VLPCB waste sorting material and energy requirements. These parameters were tested, as they 
influence specific life cycle stages without altering other system components. 
 
The results show that the approximated waste collection distance for the life cycle stage “VLPCB 
Waste Collection” is the most sensitive parameter across all materials. For LDPE, an Scoef ≥ 0.05 
was calculated for all 12 impact categories. 10 out of 12 impact categories were similarly affected 
for the green materials. This underscores that the assumptions made to approximate the waste 
collection distance are critical to the results. Future studies should aim to gather more accurate 
data on waste collection distances to reduce sensitivity and associated uncertainty. 
 
==PAGE:82==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
72 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
The extrusion process (material and energy requirements) ranked second regarding sensitivity. 
For LDPE, 6 out of 12 impact categories showed relevant Scoef. For PLA and TPS1, 4 and 5 impact 
categories were affected, respectively, 8 for TPS2. To reflect more accurate energy and material 
requirements in future studies, primary industry data regarding the VLPCB manufacturing should 
be collected.  
 
In contrast, changes in the transport distances, both to Denmark and within Denmark, showed 
minimal influence on the results, affecting only one impact category each for TPS1 and TPS2. 
This suggests that for the applied modeling and assumptions, the applied upstream and mid-
stream transport processes are less critical to the overall AESA results.  
 
An overview of all perturbations and their corresponding Scoef values is provided in Supplementary 
Information S3.2.  
 
5.2.3 Scenario-Analysis 
 
Interdependent or system-wide relevant parameters were analyzed in the scenario analysis. Ta-
ble 5-2 summarizes the five scenarios considered. In each case, the selected parameters were 
reduced by 26.67% 
 
This reduction is based on the VLPCB wall thickness assumption (see Section 4.1.1). The study 
assumes for the baseline scenario (1) a wall-thickness of 15µm, which defines both the functional 
unit and the reference flow. However, VLPCBs can also have a wall thickness below 15µm (Ball-
inger & Hann, 2016). For instance, Novamont, the Materi-Bi (TPS1) manufacturer, recommends 
a wall thickness of 10-12µm when their product is used in VLPCBs (Novamont, 2019). Scenario 
2 thus assumes a wall thickness of 11µm, corresponding to a 26.67% reduction, to assess the 
impact of lower material mass. The same reduction rate was applied to all other parameters to 
enable better comparability across scenarios. 
 
Table 5-2 - Overview of assessed scenarios

affects the total material input and propagates across all life cycle 
stages. 
3

Given the high sensitivity of the AESA results to this parameter (see 
Section 5.2.2), the VLPCB waste collection distance was reduced 
by 26.67%, to assess even lower reduction for this parameter. 
While this is not an interdependent parameter or system-wide vari-
ation, its strong influence justifies this scenario analysis. 
4

Adjustment of EoL composition: for LDPE, recycling share in-
creased, and incineration reduced by 26.67%. For green materials, 
industrial composting increased, and incineration reduced.

==PAGE:83==

Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
73 
The scenario analysis shows that none of the tested adjustments fundamentally alters the overall 
conclusion of the AESA study. All impact categories that exceeded the SoSOSEPC,FCE in the base-
line scenario remained above the thresholds across all scenarios. Therefore, an assessment of 
the alternative allocation approach was deemed unnecessary.  
 
Even in the best-case scenario, in which all optimizations were implemented simultaneously, only 
one impact category for LPDE changed its sustainability status: “Freshwater Change – Basin 
Semidry”. The environmental impacts decreased by 33% and thus dropped below the assigned 
SoSOSEPC,FCE threshold.  
 
As expected, material reduction had the most significant effect across all materials. Since almost 
all processes scale with the total material mass, a reduction of 26.67% translates proportionally 
into the environmental impacts in every impact category. The reduction of the waste collection 
distance showed the second-largest influence, with impact reductions ranging from 2.30% to 
19.95% for LDPE, 0.13% to 18.19% for PLA, 0.41% to 19.28% for TPS1, and 0.11% to 21.20% 
for TPS2 in the respective impact categories.  
 
EoL composition changes had the least influence overall. The impact reductions were generally 
low, and for specific impact categories even negative, i.e., the environmental impact increased. 
Changes ranged from -2.32% to 4.98% for LDPE, -0.12% to 2.51% for PLA, -0.11% to 8.80% for 
TPS1, and -0.13% to 5.63% for TPS2. Switching to a seemingly more sustainable EoL treatment 
option does not always lead to improvements as slight increases can be observed in specific 
impact categories. For instance, the environmental impacts increased by 2.32% for LDPE in the 
“Biogeochemical Flows: N” impact category. For PLA, slight increases were measured for the 
impact categories “Atmospheric Aerosol Loading” (-0.07%), “Biogeochemical Flows: N” (-0.01%) 
and “Stratospheric Ozone Depletion” (-0.12%). For TPS1 and TPS2, similar small negative 
changes within the same categories occurred.  
 
From these results, the following conclusions can be drawn: First, material reduction offers the 
most significant leverage for improving the environmental impacts across all impact categories 
and materials. Second, as discussed in Subsection 5.2.2, assumptions on waste collection dis-
tances are highly influential and should be based on more robust and representative data. Third, 
modifying EoL treatment compositions towards seemingly favorable EoL options (e.g., recycling 
vs. incineration) does not automatically result in better environmental performance. Lastly, in the 
current LCI modeling, EoL changes are not the most critical leverage point for improving the en-
vironmental performance of the VLPCBs based on the different material options. 
 
5.3 Uncertainty Analysis 
 
The study conducted a Monte Carlo simulation in openLCA v2.4.1 to quantify the uncertainty 
associated with the AESA results due to the cumulative effects of imprecision in the LCI modeling 
(ISO14044). The Monte Carlo simulation was chosen, as it is one of the most widely applied 
methods for doing an uncertainty analysis in LCA studies (Heijungs, 2021), and the feature is 
readily available in openLCA v2.4.1.  
The simulation approach randomly varies the LCI data across multiple iterations based on prede-
fined uncertainty distributions (GreenDelta GmbH, 2025a). A logarithmic normal distribution was 
applied for the LCI data variation. The reason is twofold: (1) the lognormal distribution is the used 
==PAGE:84==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
74 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
distribution to describe uncertainty in the ecoinvent v3.11 (APOS) database (ecoinvent, 2025a), 
and (2) using the lognormal distribution is widely adopted in LCA practice and particularly well-
suited in this context for performing Monte Carlo simulations Adams & Allacker, 2025). 
 
To perform the Monte Carlo simulation, uncertainty information in form of geometric standard 
deviation must be added to the product system (GreenDelta GmbH, 2025a). Where available, 
existing uncertainty values from the ecoinvent v3.11 (APOS) database were used, as it provides 
defined uncertainty information for most flows of its datasets (ecoinvent, 2025a; GreenDelta 
GmbH, 2025a). Missing uncertainty information for modelled processes and flows was estimated 
using the ecoinvent data quality pedigree matrix in openLCA v2.4.1 (see Supplementary Infor-
mation S3.2). OpenLCA translated the pedigree scores into geometric standard deviations to be 
used as uncertainty information in the Monte Carlo Simulation.  
 
Figure 5-4 presents the uncertainty around the main results across all impact categories. The 
figure provides (1) the deterministic26 main results (see Figure 5-1), (2) boxplots showing the 
distribution of the Monte Carlo simulation values, and (3) the 95% confidence intervals calculated 
using the Monte Carlo Simulation. It must be noted that the boxplots deviate from the standard 
visualization. The boxplot’s whiskers represent the full range of outcomes, i.e., the lowest and 
highest value. The box spans the 5th to 95th percentile, representing 90% of values. The red line 
marks the median. This presentation was chosen to better display the Monte Carlo outcome. 
Detailed results are listed in Supplementary Information S3.2. 
 
The results of the Monte Carlo simulation indicate that in 11 out of 12 assessed impact categories, 
the introduced uncertainty does not affect the original sustainability classification (see Section 
5.1). Across these categories, the full range of Monte Carlo results (i.e., from minimum to maxi-
mum) remains either entirely below or above the assigned SoSOSEPC,FCE. As a result, the overall 
sustainability classification pattern remains unchanged: LDPE complies with the assigned 
SoSOSEPC,FCE in 3 out of 12 impact categories, while the green materials remain sustainable in 2 
out of 12 impact categories. The only deviation occurs in the impact category “Land System 
Change – Global”, where a small number of TPS2 iterations exceed the assigned SoSOSEPC,FCE. 
For these iterations, the sustainability classification shifts. 
 
However, to assess the robustness of the material ranking stated in Section 5.1 (i.e., LDPE > 
TPS2 > TPS1 > PLA), the analysis of the Monte Carlo results alone is insufficient due to overlaps 
between the studied materials (see Figure 5-4). The 95% confidence intervals provide a more 
conclusive assessment. The 95% confidence intervals are presented as the error bars around the 
mean. The 95% confidence intervals do not overlap across materials in any impact category. This 
confirms the robustness of the performance ranking observed in Section 5.1. Furthermore, none 
of the confidence intervals crosses the critical ESR threshold of 1. This finding supports the con-
clusion that the sustainability classification remains robust with 95% confidence despite the un-
certainties in the LCI data (Ryberg, Bjerre, et al., 2020). Finally, this analysis confirms that none 
of the four assessed material options can be considered as absolutely sustainable. However, this 
conclusion is limited to the scope, the applied modeling, and the underlying assumptions pre-
sented in this study.  
 
26 In this context, the indication “deterministic” was adopted from Heijungs (2021) to refer to the results calculated with-
out uncertainty. 
==PAGE:85==

Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach

5.4 Discussion & Limitations 
 
This study investigates whether VLPCBs from green materials offer an environmentally superior 
option to their conventional fossil-based counterparts. To answer this question, an AESA was 
conducted, applying the PB-LCIA methodology developed by Ryberg, Owsianiak, Richardson, et 
al. (2018).  
 
The assessed materials include LDPE, which currently dominates the Danish VLPCB market, and 
three alternative green materials: (1) PLA, (2) TPS1, and a hypothetical 100% bio-based TPS2. 
PLA and TPS1 were selected as they are commercially available, food-contact safe, and domi-
nant in the Danish market food packaging application market (Werther et al., 2023). TPS2 was 
modelled to assess the environmental performance of a fully-bio-based alternative, since TPS1 
(Mater-Bi) is a fossil-bio-based material blend. 
 
The study first analyzed the national VLPCB market to ensure relevance to the Danish context. 
This included identifying the dominant material compositions, domestic and imported bag market 
shares, trade patterns, transportation modes and routes, and the EoL treatment pathways for the 
different material alternatives. Based on this market information, the LCI was modeled using 
openLCA v2.4.1 (GreenDelta GmbH, 2025b) with data from ecoinvent v3.11 (APOS) (Wernet et 
al., 2016).  
 
The study adopted a cradle-to-grave system perspective, covering all life cycle stages from raw 
material extraction to EoL treatment. The use phase was excluded in line with previous studies 
on carrier bags. Following the LCI modeling, the PB-LCIA was performed. For this step, the PB-
LCIA dataset provided by Nørrekær Lund et al. (2023) was first adapted to ensure compatibility 
with openLCA v2.4.1 and the ecoinvent v3.11 (APOS) database. 
 
The results of this AESA indicate that none of the material options can be considered absolutely 
sustainable, as all four materials exceed the assigned SoSOSEPC,FCE in most impact categories. 
This finding implies that Denmark’s 2022 VLPCB consumption cannot be sustained within the 
planetary boundaries based on the given material options, model assumptions, and allocation 
approaches. 
 
However, the results show that LDPE performed best in this AESA study. LDPE-based VLPCBs 
remained within their assigned SoSOSEPC,FCE in three out of twelve impact categories, whereas 
the green material alternatives achieved compliance in only two categories. Furthermore, LDPE 
exhibits the lowest environmental impact scores in nine out of twelve categories (see Table 5-1), 
thereby showing the least transgression of the assigned SoSOSEPC,FCE, compared to the green 
material alternatives.  
 
These findings are consistent with the overall pattern emerging in LCA literature, where fossil-
based plastics perform best for single-use applications (Gómez & Escobar, 2022; Lacerda & 
Maceno, 2023; United Nations Environment Program, 2020). For example, the AESA’s results 
are similar to the findings of Civancik-Uslu et al. (2019) and Lacerda et al. (2024). In both studies, 
the authors showed that TPS performs worse than LDPE for thicker carrier bags. Also in the 
Danish context, the previous study on shopping bags by Bisinella et al. (2018) concluded that 
LDPE outperforms the TPS alternative. 
==PAGE:87==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
77 
While the mentioned studies focused on thicker carrier bags, the study by Ballinger & Hann (2016) 
explicitly assessed VLPCBs for the European market. Also, their findings revealed that the con-
ventional fossil-based VLPCB option outperforms the green alternatives in most impact catego-
ries. In addition, their study reported similar results to those of this AESA, showing that TPS per-
forms better than PLA for VLPCBs.  
 
These insights support the implications put forward by Bachmann (2024). He argued that bio-
based and fossil-based technologies should not be treated as mutually exclusive. Instead, he 
advocates for hybrid approaches to combine the respective strengths of both systems to increase 
the sustainability of the global plastic market in the short term while moving towards absolute 
sustainability in the long term.  
 
However, this study also highlights the potential of a hypothetical 100% bio-based TPS formula-
tion. While the assessed material composition is not commercially available, the result suggests 
that future research towards 100% bio-based materials might be worth pursuing. However, more 
development is required in two key areas to realize this potential. First, further research in bio-
based material science to enhance the functional performance is needed. Second, if only virgin 
bio-based feedstocks are to be used at scale, more sustainable agricultural practices are manda-
tory (Bachmann, 2024). 
 
While various factors are discussed in the literature, the increased material demand of green 
materials for the respective studied system is often highlighted as the main driver of their inferior 
environmental performance (see e.g., Lacerda et al. (2024); Tan et al. (2023)). This is primarily 
due to differences in material density and mechanical properties. Consistent with these findings, 
this AESA also shows that, to fulfil the defined FU, approximately 1.4 times more PLA and TPS 
is required compared to LDPE. Also, Ballinger & Hann (2016) similarly identified density and re-
quired wall thickness as key drivers of environmental performance of VLPCBs. 
 
Consequently, future green material innovations could potentially reach the environmental per-
formance of fossil-based plastics if they enable reduced wall thickness while maintaining equal 
mechanical properties. However, the scenario analysis conducted as part of this study’s sensitiv-
ity analysis revealed that a realistic reduction of 26.67% to 11µm as recommended by Novamont, 
the producer of Mater-Bi (TPS1) for the use in VLPCBs, has two effects: (a) environmental 
imapcts are proportionally reduced for LDPE and the green materials, and (b) the reduction is still 
insufficient to comply with their SoSOSEPC,FCE in more than the two impact categories already 
mead. To close this gap, future green materials must aim for even lower wall-thicknesses while 
maintaining equal functionality.  
 
However, improving material efficiency alone is not sufficient. The green materials transgressed 
the assigned SoSOSEPC,FCE in all impact categories except for for “Land System Change – Global” 
and “Stratospheric Ozone Depletion”. This finding is consistent with results from Meramo et al. 
(2024) and Bachmann (2024). In addtion, Meramo et al. (2024) demonstrated that even when 
accounting for uncertainty, the transgressed impact categories remained unchanged. This AESA 
mirrors this result, as the introduced uncertainty did not alter the main results. In addition, this 
study confirms that in most impact categories, the environmental pressures are primarily driven 
by the early life cycle stages of “Raw Material Extraction and Preprocessing”. This is in line with 
the results of Bachmann (2024), who identified similar hotspots in bio-based plastic production. 
==PAGE:88==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
78 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
The performance gap between LDPE and the green alternatives is particularly pronounced in the 
impact categories “Biogeochemical Flows: P” and “Biogeochemical Flows: N”. Bachmann (2024) 
accordingly emphasizes the need for more advanced sustainable agriculture and improved nutri-
ent cycles practices. Otherwise, current farming practices inherently constrain the absolute sus-
tainability of bio-based plastics. 
 
However, at this point, it is important to emphasize again that based on the applied modeling and 
assumptions none of the investigated material options for VLPCBs can be considered absolutely 
sustainable. The overall outcome of the AESA deteriorates further when applying the alternative 
SOS allocation approach based on the ATP and FCE principles. Compared to the EPC and FCE 
allocation approach used in the main scenario, the ATP&FCE approach is approximately 1.36 
orders of magnitude more stringent, corresponding to a 23-fold reduction of the assigned SoSO-
SATP,FCE compared to the assigned SoSOSEPC,FCE. As a result, the ESR scores shift accordingly, 
LDPE remains sustainable in only two impact categories, whereas the green materials retain sus-
tainable in just one.  
 
Similar to findings from recent AESA studies (e.g., Ghani et al. (2023); Kyttä et al. (2025) and 
Masjedi et al., 2024)), this result underlines the critical influence of the chosen sharing principles 
on the AESA outcome (Bjørn et al., 2025). The results of this study are a good example to display 
that the normative decisions regarding SOS distribution can substantially affect whether a mate-
rial option can be considered sustainable within a given impact category in AESA studies. 
 
Within the academic discourse, this sensitivity to allocation assumptions has been identified as a 
key point of criticism of the AESA framework (see e.g., Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual (2024); Puig-
Samper et al. (2025) and Yang & Paulillo (2025)). The present study reinforces this critique and 
demonstrates the importance of exploring alternative SoSOS allocation approaches in future 
studies, as suggested by e.g., Bjørn et al. (2025) or Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual (2024), and the 
need for scientific consensus on allocation methodologies, especially if AESA is used in decision-
making.  
 
Nevertheless, as this AESA study applied the most commonly used sharing principles (Zhang et 
al., 2025), and demonstrates that the main findings are robust across several sensitivity and un-
certainty analyses. These insights lead to the conclusion that Denmark's 2022 VLPCB consump-
tion cannot be sustained within the planetary boundaries, given the investigated material options, 
model assumptions, and allocation approaches. 
 
These results must be interpreted with caution. Like any LCA study, this one also faces limitations, 
which can be grouped into two main categories, namely (a) limitations arising from data quality 
and (b) limitations resulting from (allocation) assumptions. First, for all LCAs, the reliability and 
representativeness of the used data pose several challenges. (Gómez & Escobar, 2022), for in-
stance, show that most reviewed studies rely on secondary data, with only 35% using primary 
data sources. The present LCA also relies on existing information from the ecoinvent v3.11 
(APOS) database. Due to this reliance, the availability and granularity of LCI data for the early life 
cycle stages of the green materials are considerably lower than for fossil-based alternatives, as. 
For example, LDPE-related data is available in region-specific market datasets (e.g., RER and 
RoW), while only global market providers are available for PLA. Similar limitations also apply to 
==PAGE:89==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
79 
the EoL treatment processes. Specific waste treatment technologies are available for LDPE, 
whereas generic biowaste processes must be used for green materials (see Section 4.2.4). 
 
A further limitation stemming from data issues is that this study exclusively assesses virgin mate-
rial. This constraint arises from the fact that the ecoinvent database, in its current version, offers 
only virgin material datasets for the considered green materials PLA and TPS1. As a result, this 
study does not capture potential environmental benefits associated with using recycled material 
for LDPE or alternative feedstock such as biowaste for green materials. This restriction is signifi-
cant in regard to circular economy strategies, for which it is central to replace virgin materials with 
alternative and recycled materials to decrease environmental impacts (Bachmann et al., 2023). 
Therefore, future research should explore alternative production routes, such as biowaste agri-
cultural residues, or other low-impact feedstocks as inputs, and test their potential against the 
planetary boundaries. 
 
The second set of limitations directly results from the absence of specific data, which made it 
necessary to rely on modeling assumptions. These assumptions were derived from previous re-
search and publicly available databases and are thus as accurate as possible given the time and 
scope of this thesis. However, as more real-world data becomes available, these assumptions 
should be refined to create more realistic results of the environmental performance of the VLPCB 
material options under Danish conditions. Such improvements are crucial for enabling more reli-
able and policy-relevant comparative assessments in the future (Ballinger & Hann, 2016). 
 
Finally, to gain a more comprehensive understanding of the absolute environmental sustainability 
of VLPCBs, future studies should (a) assess all currently defined PB processes, and (b) explore 
a broader range of allocation principles. Regarding (a), this study focused on the PB-LCIA meth-
odology initially developed by (Ryberg, Owsianiak, Richardson, et al., 2018), covering seven of 
the nine PB processes. In the context of this study, the existing PB-LCIA datasets for openLCA 
were updated. However, other scholars have adopted alternative approaches to assess the envi-
ronmental impacts of their studied system against the initially missing PB processes "Biosphere 
Integrity" and "Novel Entities". For instance, Ryberg et al. (2020) (Ryberg, Bjerre, et al., 2020) 
used complementary impact assessment methods (e.g., ReCiPe) to capture “Biosphere Integrity”, 
while (Bachmann et al., 2023) revised and expanded the PB-LCIA framework by providing up-
dated CFs and additional model refinements for this PB process. Future studies should build on 
these attempts to enable more holistic AESA results by covering all PB processes. Concerning 
(b), in line with previous AESA literature, this study finds that the choice of sharing principles 
significantly influences the results. Future research should therefore systematically evaluate al-
ternative allocation approaches to deepen the understanding of their effects on PB transgres-
sions. Such efforts would help build methodological consensus within the emerging AESA frame-
work and increase the robustness and comparability of results across case studies (Bjørn et al., 
2025; Yang & Paulillo, 2025). 
 
 
==PAGE:90==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
80 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
6. Conclusions 
“Plastic will be the main ingredient of all our grandchildren’s recipe”.  
- Anthony T. Hicks 
 
Plastic is one of the major contributors to the environmental challenges of the 21st century. As the 
quote suggests, future generations will continue to be affected by the plastic pollution generated 
in recent decades. Single-use plastic, such as VLPCBs, is a significant contributor to severe and 
lasting environmental impacts (OECD, 2025). This has prompted practitioners and researchers 
alike to find more environmentally friendly alternatives (Bala et al., 2022; Coelho & de Souza 
Henriques, 2025; Maurizzi et al., 2022; Ncube et al., 2020). As a result, a widespread assumption 
has emerged that producing VLPCBs using green materials, such as PLA and TPS, could mitigate 
many of the associated problems. However, to what extent does thorough analysis support this 
assumption? This study sought to answer that question by conducting a comprehensive LCA of 
VLPCBs within the Danish context. 
 
For this purpose, an extensive literature review was conducted (Section 3). This was followed by 
a detailed presentation of the materials and methods used in the analyses (Section 4), before the 
results were presented (Section 5). In contrast to the common assumption that green materials 
are inherently more sustainable than fossil-based material, the findings reveal a different picture. 
The study’s main results and the insights from the sensitivity and uncertainty analyses demon-
strate that none of the assessed materials can be considered absolutely sustainable under the 
assumptions and allocation methods applied. Notably, conventional LDPE outperformed the 
green material alternatives. These findings indicate that despite their favorable reputation, green 
materials do not offer a definitive solution for the future, at least in the case of VLPCBs in Den-
mark. 
 
To conclude, it is important to acknowledge what this study cannot do. It cannot provide evidence 
on whether green materials are absolutely sustainable (a) beyond the Danish context and/or (b) 
outside the specific product and material types analyzed. Based on patterns observed in preced-
ing studies, however, there is some evidence that green materials tend to perform worse than 
their fossil-based counterparts when used for single-use plastic bags (Gómez & Escobar, 2022; 
Lacerda & Maceno, 2023). Future research is needed to strengthen and expand these findings. 
First, further studies should assess green materials beyond PLA and TPS to explore whether 
other green alternatives offer improved sustainability compared to fossil-based plastics and the 
green materials already examined in this study. Second, if results show poor environmental per-
formance, additional research should focus on alternative food packaging applications beyond 
VLPCBs, fulfilling the same functional role. Finally, more studies are required that move beyond 
the binary distinction between “fossil” and “green” materials and instead explore more hybrid ap-
proaches (Bachmann et al., 2023). Understanding which combination of fossil-based and green 
materials might enable products like VLPCBs to become absolute sustainable is central. Only by 
doing so can we move closer to “ensuring that our future society is not only carbon neutral but 
also within the limits of our planet” (Paulillo & Sanyé-Mengual, 2024).

Appendix A-3 – Danish Heat Supply, 2022 
The heat demand for a Danish waste sorting plant is estimated at 0.0189MJ per kg of waste, based on Bisinella et al. (2018). The corresponding heat supply mix was 
derived from the Energy Statistics 2022 from (Danish Energy Agency, 2024; Lysgaard, 2025).

Appendix A-4 – PB-LCIA Dataset Adaptions 
This appendix section is structured according to the Earth System Processes defined in the 
Methodology Paper by Ryberg et al. (2018), which also serve as the impact categories in the 
converted PB-LCIA database. 
 
For each impact category, this annex provided the discrepancies found and adapted. Screenshots 
as visual references are added to support and clarify the observations. Adaptions were made in 
consultation with Prof. Coelho. 
 
The screenshots are divided in parts of:  
• 
Methodology Paper: Screenshots of the characterization factor (CF) table included in the 
paper. Specific environmental flows are marked if a discrepancy has been identified. 
• 
Case Study Paper - Supplementary Information: Extracts from the Excel files listing the 
CFs used in Ryberg et al.’s case study on the Danish utility company. 
 
• 
Converted PB-LCIA Database: Screenshots from OpenLCA showing the corresponding 
impact category and the CFs as implemented in the converted database. 
Furthermore, as described in Section 4.3.2, a cross-check for the correction of elementary flows 
was conducted. The whole list of adapted elementary flows and the corresponding description 
can be found in Supplementary Information S2.2. 
 
In the following pages, the adjustments for the impact categories are described. 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:123==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
XXIII 
Impact Category Climate Change – Energy imbalance at top-of-atmosphere 
 
Modification: The original impact category was titled “Energy imbalance”. To ensure consistency 
with the terminology used in the Methodology paper, the name of the impact category was 
changes to “Energy imbalance at top-of-atmosphere”. 
 
Non-methane volatile organic compounds (NMVOCs) 
This elementary flow is an example of where the initial conversion from Nørrekær Lund, Schjer-
beck, and Winther Schor (Nørrekær Lund et al., 2023) did not work properly: 
 
 
(Screenshot of the unadapted PB-LCIA dataset version) 
 
The converted dataset added new elementary flows within a new flow-hierarchy. When looking 
at the column “category“ the difference can be identified. The ecoinvent processes are built on 
flows located in the folder structure „Elementary flows/…“. However, this structure is missing. 
Accordingly in the updated version, the correct elementary flows were added (see below). 
 
 
(Screenshot of the adapted PB-LCIA dataset) 
 
==PAGE:124==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
XXIV 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
Furthermore, the methodology Paper distinguishes between three categories of NMVOCs, each 
associated with a separate characterization factor (CF): 
 
• 
Hydrocarbons 
 
• 
Partly oxidized hydrocarbons 
 
• 
Partly chlorinated hydrocarbons 
 
Each category is listed with its own CF value (highlighted in yellow below): 
 
 
 
However, in the Case Study Paper by Ryberg et al., only a single aggregated CF value of 
1.06E-12 is used for NMVOCs, without further differentiation (highlighted in purple).  
 
 
 
==PAGE:125==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
XXV 
As part of the review, the available ecoinvent flows for NMVOCs were examined. Unfortunately, 
ecoinvent does not distinguish between the different NMVOC subcategories defined in the Meth-
odology Paper. It only provides a generic NMVOC flow of unspecified origin. These flows only 
differ in terms of location, e.g., low or high population density.  
 
Given this limitation, the study chose to continue using the single CF for NMVOC emissions as 
provided in the Case Study Paper. This approach is also more conservative, as this CF corre-
sponds to the highest among the three subcategory values and would thus serve as the most 
stringent CF during interpretation. 
 
Impact Category Climate Change – Atmospheric CO2 Concentration 
 
NVMOCs 
 
Similar to the previous NMVOCs, the same issue applies to the second impact category. In the 
original dataset from the case study paper, only a single CF value for non-methane volatile or-
ganic compounds (NMVOCs) is listed: 8.07E-11 (highlighted in red).  
 
 
 
 
Methodology paper: 
 
 
 
Discrepancy was handled in the same way as before.

NMVOCs

way as for the previous impact categories. 
 
 
Impact Category – Biogeochemical Flows: P 
 
For this impact category, (Ryberg, Bjerre, et al., 2020) calculated regional specific CF values for 
their study. Accordingly, the impact category was previously called “Biogeochemical Flows: re-
gionalized P”. This nomenclature was also converted to the converted dataset. However, this 
study uses the generalized CFs for this impact category. Therefore, the name of this impact cat-
egory was adapted initially.  
 
Furthermore, in the Methodology Paper, the following characterization factors (CFs) were de-
fined for phosphate-related emissions under the Biochemical Flows – P category:

XXXVII 
Impact Category - Land System Change 
 
Note: As described in Section 4.3.1 and inn line with the findings of Nørrekær Lund et al. (2023), 
the current ecoinvent v3.11 (APOS) database lacks spatial resolution of land-change flows to 
distinguish between the different biome types mentioned by Ryberg et al. (2018). Consequently, 
only the global land system change impact category is considered in this study. 
 
However, the additionally introduced elementary flows from the original PB-LCIA dataset devel-
oped by Ryberg et al. (2018) were converted by Nørrekær Lund et al. (2023) if in future ecoinvent 
adds more detail in this context. Therefore, in the adapted PB-LCIA dataset, these flows were 
kept and corrected as explained below.  
 
Important: If future versions of ecoinvent will add spatial detail, the correct elementary flows from 
the right compartment must be added to the corresponding impact categories, as it is explained 
in Section 4.3.2. 
 
Boreal forest transformation 
 
The Methodology Paper defines four distinct characterization factors (CFs) under the Land Sys-
tem Change impact category, corresponding to the following subcategories: 
 
• 
Forest transformation 
 
• 
Boreal forest transformation 
 
• 
Temperate forest transformation 
 
• 
Tropical forest transformation 
 
 
 
However, the original dataset from the Ryberg Case Study includes only flows related to trans-
formations from boreal forest and taiga: 
 
 
 
Therefore, the converted PB-LCIA data set only includes the same flows: 
 
==PAGE:138==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
XXXVIII 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
 
 
However, due to the conversion of Nørrekær Lund et al. (2023), newly added elementary flows 
are available in their converted PB-LCIA dataset, such as, e.g. transformation, to urban, boreal 
forest and taiga. 
 
 
 
To avoid missing relevant flows, these elementary flows were added to this impact category 
(see next page).

Tropical Forest Transformation 
 
In the Ryberg case study data, flows labeled simply as “forest” were used (highlighted in red). It 
is, however, in my opinion unclear whether these generic forest flows accurately represent tropi-
cal forests. 
 
 
 
Based on this, the converted PB-LCIA data set also includes these generic forest flows (see 
next page). 
 
==PAGE:141==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
XLI 
 
 
To avoid misclassifications the CFs were adjusted to the global CF value of 1.56E-12. 
 
 
 
Important: When in future more detail is added, future studies should evaluate if this is the right 
way to do it to not double-count or miscount environmental impacts related to land-system 
change.  
==PAGE:142==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
XLII 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
Important: Reforestation – applicable for all subcategories 
 
The Methods Paper does not provide any negative characterization factor values for reforesta-
tion or forest regrowth. 
 
 
 
However, in the Ryberg case study dataset, negative CF values were assigned to reforestation 
flows for the subcategories global and temperate forest transformation (highlighted in purple), 
while no such negative CFs were included for boreal or tropical forest transformation. 
 
 
(flows from the original data set for land system change - temperate forest transformation) 
 
This raises the following question: 
 
Should negative CFs also be added for reforestation flows in the boreal and tropical subcatego-
ries, to reflect the potential environmental benefits of land-use changes that lead to forest re-
growth? 
 
Or alternatively: 
 
Should all negative CFs for reforestation be removed entirely from the dataset, to remain con-
sistent with the methods paper, which does not include such values? 
 
For this study, the negative CF values were deleted. This decision is based on the fact, that for 
potential reforestation, the same CF value as a negative was appointment. However, given the 
fact that reforestation takes decades, also for reestablishing biodiversity, etc., this study con-
cluded that this value cannot be correct. Furthermore, there is a lack of scientific information to 
this aspect.  
==PAGE:143==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach

•

•

• 
Humid 
 
 
 
In the Ryberg case study dataset, a different nomenclature is used: 
 
• 
Dry 
• 
Semi-dry 
• 
Humid 
 
 
 
Despite this variation in terminology, the underlying meaning appears consistent. Ryberg ap-
plies average annual flow values only.  
 
Therefore, the only the CF values for average annual flow are converted in the PB-LCIA dataset 
and will be used in the study.

XLVII 
Freshwater Use – Basin Humid 
 
Within this subcategory two found discrepancies were found.  
 
In the data used in the Ryberg case study, three different characterization factors (CFs) are 
used for the subcategory basin humid: 
 
• 
7.61E-14 → This value is correct and corresponds to the average annual flow as listed 
in the Methodology Paper. 
 
• 
1.51E-11 → This value is actually the CF for arid average annual flow and does not be-
long to the humid category. 
 
• 
4.14E-16 → This value is not listed or explained in the Methodology Paper and its 
source or rationale remains unclear. 
 
 
 
Nevertheless, all three values were included in the converted PB-LCIA dataset. 
 
In order to be consistent with the other basins, the CF values were adjusted to the average an-
nual flow as listed in the Methodology Paper (see below – marked in red adjusted to 7.61E-14.

LXI 
Appendix A-8 – LCI Modeling of Raw Material Extraction, Preprocessing and VLPCB Manufacturing 
Table A 9 - In- and Outputs for modelling LDPE-based VLPCB manufacturing in Denmark 
Inputs 
 
Flow 
Quantity 
Unit 
Provider 
Description 
extrusion, plastic film 
2570.23 
kg 
Extrusion, plastic film | extrusion, 
plastic film | APOS, U - RER 
This flow accounts for the inputs and outputs necessary for transform-
ing the LDPE granulates into plastic film via extrusion. The activity 
explicitly lists that the material to be converted is not included and 
must be included separately. 1 kg of the process corresponds to 
0.976kg of extruded plastic film. 
According to Appendix A-12, it is assumed that Denmark produces 
2508.54 kg of LDPE-based VLPCBs. To achieve this output, a higher 
input of the flows “extrusion, plastic film” in kg and “polyethylene, low 
density, granulate” in kg is required due to the conversion efficiency 
of the extrusion process of 0.976 kg. Consequently, both input flows 
are set to 2570.23 kg to account for process inefficiencies. 
polyethylene, low density,  
granulate 
2570.23 
kg 
Adapted Production Denmark- market 
for polyethylene, low density, granu-
late | polyethylene, low density, gran-
ulate | APOS, U (copy) - GLO 
The raw material extraction, refinery, production and trade of LDPE 
raw material in form of granulates are assumed to be globalized pro-
cesses (Bisinella et al., 2018). 
The selected market activity in ecoinvent includes all upstream pro-
cesses and flows required for the production of virgin LPDE granu-
lates. In addition, it includes the transportation of the LDPE granulates 
to its consumer, i.e. the manufacturer of the LDPE-based VLCPBs. 
Therefore, this market activity models a cradle-to-gate supply chain 
ending at the gate of the LDPE VLPCB manufacturer. Therefore, no 
separate modelling of raw material extraction, refinery, production and 
transport to the LDPE manufacturing plant is required.  
table continues on the next page 
==PAGE:162==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK 
 
LXII 
Assessing Environmental Impacts of Food Packaging: An LCA Approach 
Inputs 
 
 
 
 
 
Note: The market activity only provides virgin LDPE granulates. Ac-
cordingly, the study assumes that the material input is of 100% virgin 
plastic.  
The provider is labelled as "Adapted". The composition of input flows 
has been modified to better reflect the national supply chain of Den-
mark in the reference year 2022. This adaption is necessary as ecoin-
vent offers a global market provider for “polyethylene, low density 
granulate” with an input mix of RER (Europe) and RoW (Rest of 
World) LDPE granulates. Its default mix for 1kg LDPE granulate is as 
follows: 
(1) polyethylene production, low density, granulate | polyeth-
ylene, low density, granulate | APOS, U – RER - 
0.190635451505017 kg (ca. 19%) 
 
(2) polyethylene production, low density, granulate | polyeth-
ylene, low density, granulate | APOS, U – RoW - 
0.809364548494983 kg (ca. 81%) 
However, the import data for 2022 (see Appendix A-10) shows that 
Denmark's imports originated predominantly from Europe (RER). To 
better represent this, the input shares have been adjusted accordingly 
to: 
(3) 0.9901 kg RER (99.01%) to 0.0099 kg RoW (0.99%) 
Outputs

This flow accounts for the inputs and outputs necessary for transform-
ing the PLA granulates into plastic film via extrusion. The activity ex-
plicitly lists that the material to be converted is not included and must

According to Appendix A-12, it is assumed that Denmark produces 
3390.24 kg of PLA-based VLPCBs. To achieve this output, a higher

3473.66 
kg 
market for polylactic acid, granulate | 
polylactic acid, granulate | APOS, U - 
GLO 
The raw material extraction, refinery, production and trade of PLA raw 
material in form of granulates is assumed to be globalized processes 
(Bisinella et al., 2018). 
The selected market activity in ecoinvent includes all upstream pro-
cesses and flows required for the production of virgin PLA granulates. 
In addition, it includes the transportation of the PLA granulates to its 
consumer, i.e. the manufacturer of the PLA-based VLCPBs. There-
fore, this market activity models a cradle-to-gate supply chain ending 
at the gate of the PLA VLPCB manufacturer. Therefore, no separate 
modelling of raw material extraction, refinery, production and transport 
to the PLA manufacturing plant is required.

The selected market activity in ecoinvent includes all upstream pro-
cesses and flows required for the production of virgin TPS1 granu-
lates. In addition, it includes the transportation of the TPS1 granulates 
to its consumer, i.e. the manufacturer of the TPS1-based VLCPBs. 
Therefore, this market activity models a cradle-to-gate supply chain 
ending at the gate of the TPS1 VLPCB manufacturer. Therefore, no 
separate modelling of raw material extraction, refinery, production and 
transport to the TPS1 manufacturing plant is required.  
Outputs 
 
 
 
 
TPS1 Bag – Origin Denmark

kg 
Extrusion, plastic film | extrusion, 
plastic film | APOS, U - RoW 
This flow accounts for the inputs and outputs necessary for transform-
ing the TPS1 granulates into plastic film via extrusion. The activity ex-
plicitly lists that the material to be converted is not included and must 
be included separately. 1 kg of the process corresponds to 0.976kg of 
extruded plastic film. 
According to Appendix A-12, it is assumed that China produces 
122,246.67 kg of TPS1-based VLPCBs. To achieve this output, a 
higher input of the flows “extrusion, plastic film” in kg and “polyester-
complexed starch biopolymer” in kg is required due to the conversion 
efficiency of the extrusion process of 0.976 kg. Consequently, both 
input flows are set to 125,252.67 kg to account for process inefficien-
cies.
